Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
The Geometric Proof Against Atomism
Geometric reductio ad absurdum proving continuity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi geometric reductio proof that refutes the existence of indivisible parts and thereby establishes the continuity of bodies, a foundational premise for qiyas in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
If atoms existed, the hypotenuse would need to be both 3 and 2—Geometric absurdity proves continuity.

This passage presents a rational proof central to Hanafi Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh. By showing that the hypothesis of indivisible parts leads to geometric contradictions, the author demonstrates that bodies must be continuous and infinitely divisible. Understanding this proof allows you to see how the Hanafi school grounds certain legal analogies on metaphysical realities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Assume there exist indivisible parts (atoms) that cannot be further divided.
This is the hypothesis to be refuted.
2
Construct a right triangle
Consider a right triangle where each leg is exactly two such parts long.
The legs are composed of integer units of the indivisible part.
3
Apply the first geometric rule (al-Himari)
According to al-Himari's principle (hypotenuse is shorter than sum of legs), the hypotenuse must be less than three parts. So it cannot be three.
Triangle inequality: hypotenuse < leg1 + leg2 = 4 parts, but since both legs are 2 parts, the sum is 4; hypotenuse must be less than 4, so can be 2 or 3. The Himari argument rules out 3.
4
Apply the second geometric rule (al-Arus)
According to al-Arus's Pythagorean theorem, the square of the hypotenuse equals the sum of squares of the legs, which is 4+4=8. If the hypotenuse were 2, its square would be 4, contradicting 8. So it cannot be two.
Pythagorean theorem: c² = a² + b². Here a=2, b=2, so c²=8. If c=2, c²=4, contradiction.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Since the hypotenuse can be neither three nor two, the assumption leads to contradiction. Hence indivisible parts are false, and continuity is proven.
The reduction ad absurdum forces the rejection of the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول في إثبات التماثل على طور الحكمة لا الكلام أن الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ في الجهات باطل لأنه لو كان الجزء حقاً فلتكن زاوية قائمة كل ضلع منها جزآن، فالوتر لا يكون ثلاثة بالحماري القاضي بأن الوتر أقصر من الضلعين ومقدار الضلعين ها هنا ثلاثة أجزاء، لكون الواحد مشتركاً (ولا) يكون (اثنين) أيضاً (بالعروس ) الحاكم بأن مربع الوتر مساو المربعي الضلعين، ومربعا الضلعين ها هنا ثمانية ومربع الاثنين أربعة وبوجه آخر لو كان الوتر اثنين لكان مساوياً لواحد من الضلعين فتكون الزاويتان الموترتان لهما متساويتين، بعكس المأموني، فيلزم أن يكون في مثلث زاويتان قائمتان هذا خلف، وإذ بطل كون الوتر ثلاثة أجزاء وجزأين تعين الشق الثالث المشار إليه بقوله بل بينهما أي بين الثلاثة والاثنين (فبطل الجزء فثبت الاتصال) كما قرر في موضعه من بطلان التركيب من أجزاء غير متناهية، وحينئذ فنقول : هذا المتصل قابل للقسمة إلى جزأين متماثلين متوافقين في الحقيقة (فلزم الاتحاد حقيقة لأن (المتباينين في الحقيقة (لا يتصلان) حقيقة (بل) (يتماسان لأن الاتصال يقتضي وحدة الوجود والتشخص والاختلاف بالحقيقة يأباهما كما قال ابن سينا صاحب كتاب «الشفاء» في (الحكمة) فافهم أن هذا السانح عزيز ربما يشكل فيه بأن الانفصال يعدم ذات الاتصال ويحدث موجودان آخران من كتم العدم، فحينئذٍ لقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون المتصل الواحد هوية واحدة شخصية اتصالية هي حقيقتها وبعد طريان الانفصال يحدث حقيقتان ، أخريان ومن هذا لا يلزم الاتصال بين الأمور المتخالفة بالحقيقة هذا لعله يكون مكابرة عند الحدس الصائب فإن الانفصال وإن كان إعداماً وإيجاداً، لكن لا يحدث بعد الفصل إلا الأجسام الموافقة للكل في الحقيقة ضرورة، وإن كان ذلك مكابرة، وأيضاً نحن لا نحتاج في تقرير الكلام إلى الانقسام الكلي بل يكفي الوهمي الذي هو غير معدوم، كما لا يخفى على ذي بصيرة ثاقبة
I say in establishing similarity according to the method of wisdom, not Kalam: The indivisible part in directions is false, because if the part were real, then consider a right angle with each side two parts. Then the hypotenuse cannot be three according to al-Himari who rules that the hypotenuse is shorter than the two sides, and the measure of the two sides here is three parts, because the one is shared. Nor can it be two according to al-Arus, who rules that the square of the hypotenuse equals the sum of the squares of the two sides. The squares of the two sides here are eight, and the square of two is four. And in another way, if the hypotenuse were two, it would equal one of the sides, so the two angles subtended by them would be equal, contrary to al-Ma'muni, leading to a triangle having two right angles, which is absurd. Thus, since it is false that the hypotenuse is three parts and also false that it is two parts, the third possibility is indicated by his saying: 'rather between them' i.e., between three and two. So the part is false, and continuity is established. As established elsewhere, composition from infinite parts is false. Then we say: this continuum is divisible into two similar parts agreeing in essence. So union in essence is necessary, because two things differing in essence do not truly connect; rather they are contiguous, because connection requires unity of existence and individuation, which difference in essence precludes. As Ibn Sina, author of al-Shifa, said in the Metaphysics. Understand that this occurrence is precious. Perhaps it might be objected that separation annihilates the essence of connection and generates two new existences from the unseen of non-existence. Then one could say: it is possible that the single continuum is a single individual connected identity which is its essence, and after separation two other essences arise. From this, connection between essentially different things does not follow. This is perhaps obstinacy to correct intuition, for separation, though it is annihilation and creation, does not produce after division except bodies agreeing with the whole in essence necessarily. And if this is obstinacy, also we do not need in establishing [theological] discourse complete division; rather imaginary division suffices, which is not non-existent, as is not hidden from one of penetrating insight.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a rational proof for the continuity of bodies and the refutation of atomism (indivisible parts). The author uses a geometric argument: consider a right triangle with each leg consisting of two units (parts). The hypotenuse cannot be three (because it would then be longer than the sum of the legs, contradicting the Pythagorean inequality) nor two (because then it would equal one leg, making the triangle isosceles with impossible angles). Therefore, the hypotenuse must be between two and three, but that requires it to be composed of parts smaller than the assumed indivisible units. Thus, indivisible parts are impossible, and the body is continuous (متصل). Once continuity is established, the author notes that division yields parts that are similar in essence, implying that the continuum is essentially one. He cites Ibn Sina (Avicenna) to support that true connection requires unity of existence, which differing essences cannot achieve. A potential objection about separation creating new essences is dismissed as obstinacy, and the author concludes that for theological purposes, imaginary division is sufficient. This argument is crucial in Hanafi Kalam and usul for establishing the reality of bodies and the basis for certain legal analogies.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza): The indivisible part (atom) which is argued to be impossible.
الاتصال (al-ittisal): Continuity, the property of being unbroken and divisible without limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the geometric proof about the right triangle demonstrate?
A) The hypotenuse is always shorter than the legs
B) Indivisible parts must exist
C) Indivisible parts are impossible, so bodies are continuous
D) The triangle has two right angles

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses a geometric reductio ad absurdum to refute atomism and establish the continuity of bodies, a crucial metaphysical foundation for legal analogy.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

7 min
From Continuity to Essential Unity for Legal Analogy
Deriving essential unity from continuity and its implications for qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the continuity of bodies implies that divisible parts share the same essence, and how this principle justifies the validity of qiyas (analogy) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Continuity demands essential unity — only things of the same essence can truly connect; differing essences merely touch.

After establishing the continuity of bodies, the passage argues that any two parts of a continuum must be identical in essence. This conclusion is vital for legal analogy because analogical reasoning requires a shared effective cause (illah) that is essentially the same across cases. Understanding this metaphysical grounding helps you see why the Hanafi school considers qiyas a valid source of law.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conclusion of continuity
We have proven that bodies are continuous (mutasil) and infinitely divisible.
This is the starting point for the next argument.
2
Consider the division of a continuum
If we divide a continuous body, the resulting parts are similar in essence because they come from the same continuous whole.
The author asserts that division yields parts that are mithmaylan (similar) and mutawafiqan fi al-haqiqah (agreeing in reality).
3
Apply the principle of connection (ittisal)
True connection (ittisal) requires unity of existence and individuation. Two things that differ in essence cannot truly connect; they can only be contiguous (yamassan).
Ibn Sina's definition: ittisal demands that the connected entities share one existence.
4
Derive essential unity
Since the continuous body is truly connected (by ittisal), all its parts must have the same essence. Therefore, the whole is essentially one.
Thus, any two parts of a continuous body are identical in essence.
5
Link to legal analogy
In qiyas, the effective cause (illah) must be shared by the original case (asl) and the new case (far'). If the two cases are essentially the same, the analogy is valid. The metaphysical unity of continuous bodies underpins the possibility of such shared essences.
This provides a rational foundation for the validity of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول في إثبات التماثل على طور الحكمة لا الكلام أن الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ في الجهات باطل لأنه لو كان الجزء حقاً فلتكن زاوية قائمة كل ضلع منها جزآن، فالوتر لا يكون ثلاثة بالحماري القاضي بأن الوتر أقصر من الضلعين ومقدار الضلعين ها هنا ثلاثة أجزاء، لكون الواحد مشتركاً (ولا) يكون (اثنين) أيضاً (بالعروس ) الحاكم بأن مربع الوتر مساو المربعي الضلعين، ومربعا الضلعين ها هنا ثمانية ومربع الاثنين أربعة وبوجه آخر لو كان الوتر اثنين لكان مساوياً لواحد من الضلعين فتكون الزاويتان الموترتان لهما متساويتين، بعكس المأموني، فيلزم أن يكون في مثلث زاويتان قائمتان هذا خلف، وإذ بطل كون الوتر ثلاثة أجزاء وجزأين تعين الشق الثالث المشار إليه بقوله بل بينهما أي بين الثلاثة والاثنين (فبطل الجزء فثبت الاتصال) كما قرر في موضعه من بطلان التركيب من أجزاء غير متناهية، وحينئذ فنقول : هذا المتصل قابل للقسمة إلى جزأين متماثلين متوافقين في الحقيقة (فلزم الاتحاد حقيقة لأن (المتباينين في الحقيقة (لا يتصلان) حقيقة (بل) (يتماسان لأن الاتصال يقتضي وحدة الوجود والتشخص والاختلاف بالحقيقة يأباهما كما قال ابن سينا صاحب كتاب «الشفاء» في (الحكمة) فافهم أن هذا السانح عزيز ربما يشكل فيه بأن الانفصال يعدم ذات الاتصال ويحدث موجودان آخران من كتم العدم، فحينئذٍ لقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون المتصل الواحد هوية واحدة شخصية اتصالية هي حقيقتها وبعد طريان الانفصال يحدث حقيقتان ، أخريان ومن هذا لا يلزم الاتصال بين الأمور المتخالفة بالحقيقة هذا لعله يكون مكابرة عند الحدس الصائب فإن الانفصال وإن كان إعداماً وإيجاداً، لكن لا يحدث بعد الفصل إلا الأجسام الموافقة للكل في الحقيقة ضرورة، وإن كان ذلك مكابرة، وأيضاً نحن لا نحتاج في تقرير الكلام إلى الانقسام الكلي بل يكفي الوهمي الذي هو غير معدوم، كما لا يخفى على ذي بصيرة ثاقبة

This lengthy passage presents a rational proof for the continuity of bodies and the refutation of atomism (indivisible parts). Key Terms
الاتصال (al-ittisal): True connection requiring essential unity; as opposed to tamass (mere contact).
التماس (al-tamass): Mere contiguity or touching, without essential connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do two things differing in essence not truly connect?
A) Because they cannot share the same physical space
B) Because true connection requires unity of existence and individuation
C) Because the Quran prohibits such connection
D) Because they would cancel each other out

Key Takeaway
The continuity of bodies implies essential unity of parts, which is a necessary condition for the validity of qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Responding to Objections: The Sufficiency of Imaginary Division
Refutation of an objection using imaginary division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi refutation of the objection that continuity is lost upon division, and explain why imaginary division is sufficient for establishing the essential unity of a continuum.

Core Idea
Even if division creates new essences, imaginary division suffices to prove essential unity—actual division is not needed.

The passage anticipates an objection: if a continuous body is separated into two parts, those parts might have different essences, so the principle of essential unity fails. The author responds by dismissing this as obstinacy and further argues that for theological and legal purposes, we do not need actual division; imaginary division is enough to demonstrate that the continuum is essentially one. This lesson sharpens your ability to defend Hanafi metaphysical claims against counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that when a continuum is split, the resulting parts are new existences with potentially different essences, so the original whole is not essentially one.
This objection challenges the conclusion that all parts share the same essence.
2
Understand the author's first response
The author calls this 'obstinacy' (mukabara) and asserts that after division, the parts necessarily agree with the whole in essence because they were part of that whole.
The author relies on intuitive insight (hadas sa'ib) that the parts cannot be entirely different.
3
Analyze the second response: imaginary division
Even if one insists on the objection, the author offers a stronger reply: we do not need actual division (al-inqisam al-kulli) to establish essential unity; imaginary division (al-wahmi) is sufficient.
Imaginary division is a thought experiment that does not create new entities but reveals the inherent divisibility and uniformity of the continuum.
4
Apply the principle to usul
In usul al-fiqh, imaginary division is accepted because it does not rely on actual physical events. The essential unity of the continuum is a rational truth known through reason, not contingent on empirical division.
This reinforces the use of rational proofs (dalil 'aqli) in establishing foundational premises for legal reasoning.
5
Conclude the defense
Thus, the objection does not undermine the Hanafi position. Continuity and essential unity are established through reason, and imaginary division is a legitimate tool for demonstration.
This illustrates the Hanafi confidence in rational proof.

Source Text

Thus, since it is false that the hypotenuse is three parts and also false that it is two parts, the third possibility is indicated by his saying: 'rather between them' i.e., between three and two. So the part is false, and continuity is established. As established elsewhere, composition from infinite parts is false. Then we say: this continuum is divisible into two similar parts agreeing in essence. So union in essence is necessary, because two things differing in essence do not truly connect; rather they are contiguous, because connection requires unity of existence and individuation, which difference in essence precludes. Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a rational proof for the continuity of bodies and the refutation of atomism (indivisible parts). The author uses a geometric argument: consider a right triangle with each leg consisting of two units (parts). The hypotenuse cannot be three (because it would then be longer than the sum of the legs, contradicting the Pythagorean inequality) nor two (because then it would equal one leg, making the triangle isosceles with impossible angles). Therefore, the hypotenuse must be between two and three, but that requires it to be composed of parts smaller than the assumed indivisible units. Thus, indivisible parts are impossible, and the body is continuous (متصل). Once continuity is established, the author notes that division yields parts that are similar in essence, implying that the continuum is essentially one. He cites Ibn Sina (Avicenna) to support that true connection requires unity of existence, which differing essences cannot achieve. A potential objection about separation creating new essences is dismissed as obstinacy, and the author concludes that for theological purposes, imaginary division is sufficient. Key Terms
الانقسام الوهمي (al-inqisam al-wahmi): Imaginary division; mental partition of a continuum without physical actualization.
المكابرة (al-mukabara): Obstinacy; the act of denying an obvious truth out of stubbornness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the Sahibayn recommended it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends the essential unity of continuous bodies by arguing that imaginary division is sufficient proof, thereby neutralizing objections based on actual physical division.

Proof (Dalil) and Syllogism in Hanafi Usul
Defining proof and explaining the structure of syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of proof (dalil) and identify the structural requirements for a valid syllogism in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Proof leads to knowledge; syllogism requires an affirmative minor and a universal major.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author defines 'proof' (dalil) in Hanafi legal methodology, distinguishing it from mere probable indicators (amara). He then elaborates on the logical structure of syllogism (qiyas) as the primary form of rational inference, setting out the conditions for a valid categorical syllogism. Understanding this foundational concept is essential for grasping how Hanafi jurists construct legal arguments from both textual and rational sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of dalil
Locate the sentence starting with 'ثم الدليل' and note the key phrase 'ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى مطلوب خبري'. Recognize that this definition includes both certain (qat'i) and probable (zanni) proof, with the latter called amara.
2
Understand the structure of production (intaj)
Read the section starting with 'والانتاج مبني على التثليث'. Note that a valid inference requires two extremes and a middle term, hence two premises are necessary. This is the basis of syllogism.
3
Distinguish broad vs. restricted proof
The author mentions that logicians define proof as two statements yielding a third, which includes syllogism, induction, and analogy. However, some restrict proof to syllogism alone. Note the author's critique that induction and analogy also entail likelihood if premises are admitted.
The Hanafi position is inclusive; both certain and probable proofs are acceptable in legal reasoning.
4
Learn the conditions for a valid categorical syllogism
Find the sentence: 'فلا بد من إيجاب الصغرى وكلية الكبرى'. The minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. This ensures necessary entailment.
The text also discusses an exception from 'al-Tahrir' regarding equality, which the author refutes as relying on an extraneous premise.
5
Evaluate the critique of the equality case
The author argues that when the major premise involves equal terms, a negative minor may seem to work, but the inference is not essential; it depends on the extra premise that equals share the same ruling. This shows the importance of essential versus accidental validity.
This critical point refines the understanding of syllogistic validity.

And when he finished with the definiendum, he began on 'the proof' and said: Then the proof, in our terminology, is that by which sound reflection can reach an informative sought-after (matalub khabari) like 'the world is created' (which is the minor term in logic). It may be particularized to the certain, so proof on this is that by which sound reflection can reach a certain informative sought-after. And the probable is called amara. Then he began to explain the method of reflection and said: Production (intaj) is based on threefold division, for the sought-after must have two extremes, and they are not sufficient; rather there must be a middle term between them, so two premises are necessary. From here, because production depends on two premises, the logician said that proof is two statements (i.e., two propositions) (using the more general for the more specific) from which another statement (i.e., another proposition) results. And it includes syllogism, induction, analogy, equality syllogism, and others in which there is implication via an extraneous premise. And it may be said: Proof is a statement composed of two statements that essentially entails another statement, so it is restricted to syllogism. The logicians said that induction and analogy do not entail anything, and there is apparent consideration in this, because the nature of analogy and syllogism is one; the outcome of analogy is that the cause of this ruling exists in another matter, so the ruling necessarily exists if admitted, just as syllogism does not entail anything unless its premises are admitted. The probability comes only due to the probability of the premises as in rhetorical syllogism, so it is better not to exclude them. And for it (syllogism) there are five figures that are close in production; as for non-close ones, they are many like the fourth figure and the forms of conditional conjunctive syllogism, and they are not needed in most cases. It is better that one knows the ruling (affirmative or negative) for all individuals of a thing (this is the content of the major premise) then one knows the existence of that thing (subject) for the other (subject) in whole or in part (i.e., for every individual of it or some) (this is the content of the minor premise), then from both necessarily follows the existence of that ruling for the other, affirmatively or negatively, likewise, necessarily. So the minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal. What is in al-Tahrir is that only in equality of the two extremes of the major premise, a negative minor suffices, because negation of one equal entails negation of the other. But this is not correct because this production is not essential but by noting that the ruling of equals is one, and this is an extraneous premise. This objection only applies if the restriction was by the essence, otherwise not. And it was objected that the syllogism composed of a negative minor and a positive major is absolutely...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage introduces the definition of proof (dalil) in Hanafi usul. The author distinguishes between certain proof (dalil qat'i) and probable indicator (amara). Then he discusses the structure of logical proof, emphasizing that production (intaj) requires three terms and two premises. He defines the logician's proof as two premises yielding a conclusion, which includes syllogism, induction, analogy, and others. However, some restrict proof to syllogism alone, arguing that induction and analogy do not entail certainty. The author counters that analogy and syllogism are structurally similar; both require acceptance of premises, and probability arises from the premises, not the form. He then outlines the conditions for a valid categorical syllogism: affirmative minor, universal major. He critiques a view from al-Tahrir that allows negative minor in cases of equality, noting that such reasoning relies on an extraneous premise (the rule that equals have the same ruling). This discussion is foundational for understanding how Hanafi jurists use logical reasoning in legal deduction.

Key Terms
دليل (dalil): That by which sound reflection can reach an informative sought-after, whether certain or probable.
أمارة (amara): A proof that leads only to probable knowledge (zann).
قياس (qiyas): A syllogism composed of two premises that necessarily entail a conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both premises must be affirmative and universal.
B) The minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal.
C) The minor premise must be negative and the major particular.
D) Only the major premise needs to be universal.

Key Takeaway
Proof in Hanafi usul is a broad concept including both certain and probable; valid syllogism requires an affirmative minor and a universal major.

Defining al-Dalil: Evidence and Logical Inference in Hanafi Usul
Definition and defense of Hanafi view of evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of al-dalil in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from al-amarah, and explain why the Hanafi school includes analogy (tamthil) as a form of evidence.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, al-dalil includes both certain and probable evidence, and analogy is valid because it entails a conclusion when premises are granted.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defines a foundational term in usul al-fiqh: al-dalil (evidence). It clarifies that evidence can be certain (qat'i) or probable (zanni), and explains the logical structure of inference. The author then addresses a dispute with logicians who restrict evidence to syllogism, arguing that analogy (tamthil) is equally valid in Hanafi methodology. Understanding this definition is crucial for recognizing how Hanafi jurists derive rulings from sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-dalil
Locate the passage's definition: 'that by which, through correct reflection, one can arrive at a desired informative conclusion.' Note that it includes both certain and probable conclusions.
This is the broad definition used in Hanafi usul.
2
Distinguish between qat'i and zanni evidence
The passage mentions that evidence can be specified as certain (qat'i) and that probable evidence is called 'amarah' (sign). Internalize this terminology.
Al-amarah is a subclass of al-dalil in Hanafi usage.
3
Understand the tripartite nature of intaj (production)
The author explains that logical production requires two premises and a middle term. This is the basis for all inference, not just syllogism.
The 'triplicity' refers to the two extremes and the middle term.
4
Compare broad and narrow definitions of al-dalil
The logicians define al-dalil narrowly as a statement that intrinsically entails another (syllogism only). The Hanafi definition is broader, including induction and analogy.
The key is 'intrinsic entailment' versus entailment via granted premises.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi defense of tamthil
The author argues that tamthil (analogy) works like syllogism: if the shared cause is present, the ruling follows necessarily. Any uncertainty arises from premises, not the form of reasoning.
This supports the validity of qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ عن المعرف شرع في «الدليل» فقال : (ثم الدليل) في اصطلاحنا (ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى مطلوب خبري كالعالم وهو الأصغر باصطلاح المنطق وقد يخص بالقطعي فالدليل على هذا ما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى مطلوب خبري قطعي (ويسمى الظنّي) أي ما يمكن التوصل فيه إلى خبري ظني (أمارة) ثم شرع في بيان طريق النظر فقال : (والانتاج مبني على التثليث إذ لا بد للمطلوب من الطرفين ولا يكفيان بل لا بد من) واسطة) بينهما (فوجب المقدمتان، ومن ههنا أي من أجل أن الإنتاج موقوف على المقدمتين (قال : المنطقي هو أي الدليل (قولان) أي قضيتان إطلاقاً للأعم على الأخص (يكون عنه قول آخر) أي قضية أخرى (وهو يتناول) القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل وقياس المساواة وغيرها مما فيه لزوم بواسطة مقدمة أجنبية (وقد يقال الدليل قول مؤلف من قولين يستلزم لذاته قولاً آخر، فيختص بالقياس) قال أهل المنطق الاستقراء والتمثيل لا يلزم منهما شيء، وفيه نظر ظاهر، لأن شأن التمثيل والقياس واحد، فإن حاصل التمثيل أن علة هذا الحكم موجودة في مادة أخرى، فيلزم وجوب وجود الحكم لو سلم، كما أن القياس لا يلزم منه شيء إلا إذا سلم مقدماته، وإنما تجيء الظنية فيه لأجل ظنية المقدمات كما في القياس الخطابي فالأولى أن لا يخرج
When he finished with the definiendum, he began with 'the evidence' and said: 'Then the evidence' in our terminology is 'that by which, through correct reflection, one can arrive at a desired informative conclusion' like 'the world' (which is the minor term in logic). It may be specified as certain: so evidence in this sense is that by which, through correct reflection, one can arrive at a certain informative conclusion. And what is called 'probable' (i.e., that by which one can arrive at a probable informative conclusion) is called 'sign' (amarah). Then he began to explain the method of reflection and said: 'Production is based on triplicity, because the desired conclusion requires two extremes, and they are not sufficient; rather there must be a middle term between them, so two premises are necessary.' Hence, because production depends on two premises, he said: 'The logician says: evidence is two statements (i.e., two propositions, using the more general for the more specific) from which another statement (i.e., another proposition) follows.' And it includes syllogism, induction, analogy, equality syllogism, and others that involve entailment via an extrinsic premise. It is sometimes said that evidence is a statement composed of two statements that intrinsically entail another statement, so it is restricted to syllogism. The logicians said: induction and analogy do not entail anything. There is obvious reflection on this, because the nature of analogy and syllogism is one: indeed, the outcome of analogy is that the cause of this ruling exists in another matter, so the necessity of the ruling follows if granted, just as nothing follows from syllogism unless its premises are granted. The probability in it arises only from the probability of the premises, as in rhetorical syllogism. So it is better not to exclude it.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'الدليل' (evidence) in usul al-fiqh. It begins by setting out the technical meaning: something that, when correctly reflected upon, leads to a desired informative result. It distinguishes between certain evidence (qat'i) and probable evidence (zanni), which is also called 'amarah'. The passage then explains the concept of logical production (الإنتاج), emphasizing the need for two premises (mugaddimatayn) with a middle term. It discusses the differing definitions of al-dalil among logicians: a broad definition includes induction (istisna') and analogy (tamthil), while a narrow definition restricts it to syllogism (qiyas) that entails a conclusion intrinsically. The author sides with the broad definition, arguing that tamthil is essentially similar to qiyas in requiring granted premises and that probability arises from premises, not the form. Therefore, it is better not to exclude tamthil from being considered evidence.

Key Terms
الدليل (al-dalil): What can lead, through correct reflection, to a desired informative conclusion.
الأمارة (al-amarah): A probable evidence that leads to a probable conclusion.
الإنتاج (al-intaj): Logical production: deriving a conclusion from two premises with a middle term.
التمثيل (al-tamthil): An argument by analogy where the ruling of one case is applied to another due to a shared cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is also called al-amarah.
B) Because it involves the same type of entailment as qiyas when premises are granted.
C) Because it always yields certain knowledge.
D) Because logicians include it in their definition.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, evidence (dalil) is broadly defined to include both certain and probable forms, and analogy (tamthil) is valid because its logical structure mirrors that of syllogism when premises are granted.

The Five Productive Forms of Syllogism in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of the five immediate productive forms of syllogism with objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five productive forms of syllogism and explain the Hanafi conditions for their productivity.

Core Idea
A productive syllogism requires an affirmative minor and a universal major in the first form; other forms revert to this via logical transformations.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into the logical structure of syllogism (qiyas) as used in Hanafi legal reasoning. Understanding these five forms is essential for constructing sound analogical arguments. The author not only lays out the forms but also addresses subtle objections that clarify the conditions for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first form (Barbara) and its conditions
The author states that the first productive form requires a universal major premise and an affirmative minor premise. This yields a necessary conclusion.
The conditions are strict: universal major and affirmative minor.
2
Analyze the objection about the negative minor with a major that is affirmative with a negative subject
An objection claims that a syllogism like 'A is not B; everything not-B is C; therefore A is C' is productive without an affirmative minor. The author explains why this is not a valid first-form syllogism unless the minor is treated as an affirmative negation (e.g., 'A is non-B').
This highlights the need for careful logical analysis.
3
Understand the second form and its reduction to the first
The second form uses opposite judgments. By contraposition, it can be reduced to the first form, but the author insists it is independently productive.
The truth of the reduction does not negate its intrinsic productivity.
4
Grasp the third form producing a particular conclusion
The third form involves knowing that two things meet or do not meet in a third subject. The conclusion is always particular (not universal).
This form is often overlooked but appears in legal reasoning.
5
Recognize modus ponens and modus tollens in the fourth conditional form
The fourth form deals with conditional propositions: placing the antecedent entails the consequent (modus ponens), and lifting the consequent entails lifting the antecedent (modus tollens). Affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid.
An objection about impossible consequents is addressed by emphasizing the universal nature of logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

التقاء ذينك الأمرين الثابتين لثالث في هذا الثالث فيلزم ثبوت واحد من الأمرين لبعض الآخر أو يعلم ثبوت أمر له أي لثالث مع عدم ثبوت الآخر له لذلك الثالث فيعلم عدم التقائهما فيه فيلزم صدق سلب هذا الآخر عن لزوم بينهما هذا خلف ولا عكس أي لا ينتج وضع التالي وضع المقدم الجواز أعمية اللازم فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم الأخص والرفع بالعكس أي ينتج رفع التالي رفع المقدم وإلا لزم تخلف الملزوم عن اللازم فلا لزوم، ولا ينتج رفع المقدم رفع التالي لجواز أخصية الملزوم ، فلا يلزم من ارتفاعه ارتفاع لازمه الأعم وأورد منع استلزام الرفع الرفع أي منع استلزام رفع اللازم رفع الملزوم لجواز استحالة انتفاء اللازم فإذا وقع هذا الانتفاء المحال جاز عدم بقاء اللزوم وكيف لا والمحال يجوز أن يستلزم محالاً فلا يلزم انتفاء الملزوم على هذا التقدير أقول في الجواب اللزوم حقيقة امتناع الانفكاك في جميع الأوقات والتقادير لأن اللزوم هنا كلي ف وقت الانفكاك وهو وقت عدم بقاء اللزوم داخل فيه فيرجع إلى منع صدق اللزوم وقد فرض هذا خلف فتدبر وفيه أنه قد تقرر في المنطق أن المعتبر في كلية الشرطية اللزوم على التقادير الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم ويجوز أن يكون هذا التقدير مستحيل الاجتماع، فلا يرجع إلى منع صدق الشرطية، وأيضاً قد بين في زبر المتأخرين الشرطية الجزئية مع الاستثناء الكلي ينتج الرفع
And it (i.e., the syllogism) has five forms close in production. As for those not close, they are many, like the fourth figure and the forms of conjunctive conditional syllogism, which are not needed in most cases. The first: that one knows a judgment, affirmative or negative, for every individual of a thing (this is the content of the major premise); then one knows the existence of that thing (i.e., the subject) for the other subject, wholly or partially, i.e., for every individual of it or some of it (this is the content of the minor premise); then from both necessarily follows the existence of that judgment for the other, affirmative or negative, similarly. Thus the affirmativeness of the minor and the universality of the major are necessary. What is in al-Tahrir except in the case of equality of the two extremes of the major: the negative minor suffices, for the negation of one of two equals entails the negation of the other. But that is nothing, because this production is not by itself but by noting that the judgment of equals is one, and this is an extrinsic premise. This objection only applies if it were conditioned by itself, otherwise not. And an objection was raised: a syllogism composed of a negative minor and a major that is affirmative with a negative subject is productive despite the absence of an affirmative minor, like our statement: 'It is not B' and 'Everything that is not B is A' yields 'It is A'. The answer: negation as pure removal and the affirmative knot in the major does not lack noting existence (by possibility or absoluteness), because the meaning of the proposition is that what the title applies to is such or not such. If you note it (O objector) in the minor as well, then it is not negation but an affirmative negation, and the minor becomes modified or with negative predicate. Otherwise, you (objector) note existence, then there is no inclusion of the minor under the middle, and that is only by noting an extrinsic premise, so understand. The second form: that one knows a judgment for every individual of a thing (this is the content of the major), and one knows its opposite, i.e., the opposite of that judgment, affirmative or negative, for the other wholly or partially (this is the content of the minor); then from it one knows the negation of that thing from the other, similarly, wholly or partially, upon reflection. For by reversing the major, it reverts to the first. The truth is that production of this form is also necessary, because two opposite judgments cannot be for one thing, so the essences of the minor and major must differ, so the negation of the major of the essence of the minor is necessarily true. The place for full discussion is another context. What is in al-Mukhtasar that there is no production except from the first because the remaining forms revert to it by reversal, so they revolve with the first in existence and non-existence, is a claim without proof. How so? For the conclusion is necessary for both because the entailment is not due to an extrinsic premise but by itself; it is possible to be with multiple, and revolving with the first in existence and non-existence does not contradict it, i.e., does not contradict the entailment not being due to an extrinsic premise. The third form: that one knows the existence of two things for a third subject, and one of them (i.e., one of the two judgments) is universal; then one knows their meeting in it, i.e., one knows the meeting of those two things established for the third in this third; then the existence of one of the two things for some of the other follows. Or one knows the existence of a thing for it (i.e., for the third) along with the non-existence of the other for it (for that third), so one knows their non-meeting in it; then the truth of the negation of that other from some of the first thing follows. Thus the consequent is only particular, affirmative or negative, as appears with the slightest reflection. The fourth form: that one establishes a necessary connection between two things; then placing the antecedent entails placing the consequent, otherwise the existence of the antecedent without the consequent would occur and there would be no connection between them—this is a reductio ad absurdum. And not the converse, i.e., placing the consequent does not entail placing the antecedent, because the necessary is more general; so its realization does not entail realization of the more specific necessary. And lifting is the reverse, i.e., lifting the consequent entails lifting the antecedent, otherwise the necessary would be left behind without the necessary, so no connection. And lifting the antecedent does not entail lifting the consequent, because the necessary may be more specific; so its removal does not entail removal of its more general necessary. And an objection was raised denying the implication of lifting for lifting, i.e., denying that lifting the necessary entails lifting the necessary, because the non-existence of the necessary may be impossible. So if this impossible non-existence occurs, the connection may cease. Why not? For the impossible may entail an impossible, so the non-existence of the necessary is not entailed on this assumption. I say in response: Connection is really the impossibility of separation in all times and all conditions, because connection here is universal. So the time of separation, which is the time when the connection ceases, is included in it, so it reverts to denying the truth of the connection, and this is assumed as a reductio ad absurdum, so reflect. And there is that it has been established in logic that the consideration in the universality of the conditional is the connection on all possible conditions together with the antecedent, and it is possible that this condition is impossible of togetherness, so it does not revert to denying the truth of the conditional. Also, it has been explained in the writings of the later scholars that the particular conditional with the universal exception produces lifting.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the five immediate productive forms of syllogism (qiyas). The author first outlines the first form (Barbara in Aristotelian logic): a universal major premise and an affirmative minor premise yield a universal conclusion necessarily. He then addresses an objection from al-Tahrir: if the two extremes of the major are equal (convertible), a negative minor might suffice, but the author argues this adds an extraneous premise. Another objection is about a syllogism with a negative minor and a major that is affirmative with a negative subject (e.g., 'It is not B; everything not-B is A; therefore it is A'). The author responds by analyzing the logical form: the negative minor actually involves a tacit affirmation of a modified predicate, so the minor becomes effectively affirmative or the inclusion fails without an extrinsic premise. The second form uses opposite judgments and by contraposition reduces to the first, but the author insists it is independently productive. The third form produces a particular conclusion from the meeting or non-meeting of two things in a third. The fourth form involves conditional propositions: modus ponens and modus tollens, with the caveat that denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent are invalid. An objection is raised against modus tollens when the consequent is impossible; the author defends the universal nature of logical necessity, arguing that if the consequent is impossible, the connection itself is false. The discussion ends with a note on particular conditionals and universal exception.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Syllogism: a compound of two premises that intrinsically entail a conclusion. Here, specifically the categorical syllogism with five productive forms.
الإنتاج (al-intaj): The production of a conclusion from premises necessarily.
الصغرى (al-sughra): The minor premise; the premise that contains the minor term.
الكبرى (al-kubra): The major premise; the premise that contains the major term.
المقدمة الأجنبية (al-muqaddimah al-ajnabiyyah): An extrinsic premise not originally part of the syllogism but needed for the conclusion.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Necessary connection; the relationship that makes the conclusion follow from the premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required for the first form of syllogism to be productive?
A) An affirmative minor and a universal major.
B) A negative minor and a particular major.
C) Any two premises with a middle term.
D) A universal minor and an affirmative major.

Key Takeaway
The first form of syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise; other forms either reduce to it or require special conditions.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
All Five Syllogistic Forms Are Productive in Hanafi Legal Reasoning
Explains the five forms of categorical syllogisms and their productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five syllogistic forms, explain why each is productive, and apply the first form to a Hanafi qiyas example.

Core Idea
All five syllogistic forms yield valid conclusions; the first form has no monopoly on productivity.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defends the productivity of all five forms of categorical syllogisms against the claim that only the first form is productive. Understanding this logical foundation is crucial for mastering qiyas in Hanafi usul, as every valid analogy reduces to one of these forms. The author walks through each form, showing how the necessity of the conclusion is intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the First Form and the Objection about Negative Premises
Identify the first form (two categorical premises with a common middle term) and the objection that a negative major and affirmative minor may not produce a conclusion. Note the author's resolution: the negation is considered as an affirmation of negation, preserving productivity.
The first form is the classic Barbara (AAA) or its valid variations.
2
Understand the Second Form and Its Reduction to the First
Recognize that the second form has a universal major and its opposite in the minor, leading to the negation of the major term from the minor. The author shows it reduces to the first by conversion of the major premise, but also argues its productivity is immediate.
This form corresponds to the syllogism 'All A are B; No C is B; therefore No C is A' (Cesare) or similar.
3
Grasp the Third Form and Its Particular Conclusion
The third form involves two predicates affirmed of a third subject, yielding a particular conclusion (e.g., Some A are B). The author explains that the conclusion is only particular, whether affirmative or negative.
This form is like 'All A are B; Some A are C; therefore Some B are C' (Darapti).
4
Examine the Fourth Form (Conditional Syllogism) and the Modus Ponens/Tollens
The fourth form is the conditional syllogism (If P then Q). It produces valid inferences: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). The author defends the absolute necessity of the connection, even if the consequent is impossible.
This is crucial for qiyas where the efficacy ('illa) is the antecedent.
5
Note the Fifth Form and the Overall Conclusion
The fifth form is briefly mentioned as a conditional with a universal exception. The author concludes that all forms are productive and that the first form is not uniquely valid, contrary to the claim in al-Mukhtasar.
The fifth form is the 'disjunctive syllogism' or particular conditional.

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the five forms of categorical syllogisms, explaining that all of them are productive of conclusions, contrary to the claim that only the first form is productive. The author first discusses the first form and a potential objection regarding negative premises, showing that a negative major with an affirmative minor can still yield a conclusion when properly understood. Then the second form (where the major establishes a universal ruling and the minor its opposite) is shown to reduce to the first by conversion, though its productivity is immediate. The third form involves two predicates for a single subject, yielding a particular conclusion. The fourth form is the conditional syllogism (modus ponens and modus tollens), with a discussion of objections regarding the necessity of the connection when the consequent is impossible. The author defends the classical view that necessity is absolute, covering all times and estimations. The fifth form is mentioned briefly at the end, though the detailed discussion is cut off. This analysis is foundational for understanding how reasoning (nazar) operates in Islamic legal theory, as usul scholars often employ syllogistic logic to derive rulings.

Key Terms
الملازمة (al-mulāzama): The necessary connection between two propositions, such that one entails the other.
اللزوم (al-luzūm): Necessity; the relation of entailment between antecedent and consequent.
الصورة (al-ṣūra): A form or figure of a syllogism, determined by the position of the middle term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It requires an extraneous premise to be productive.
D) It only produces a particular conclusion.

Key Takeaway
All five syllogistic forms are intrinsically productive; the claim that only the first form is valid is rejected in Hanafi logical tradition.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Sadr al-Shariah's Three Premises on Causality and Choice
Rational proof for choice within causal necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Sadr al-Shariah's three premises and explain how they establish the possibility of choice (tarjih) despite the necessity of the act.

Core Idea
Choice is proved by showing that voluntary acts arise from a cause that includes non-existent relations, preserving necessity without determinism.

This passage is a dense rational argument from Sadr al-Shariah aimed at reconciling causal necessity with free choice. He lays down four premises; we cover the first three. Understanding these premises is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on how human acts are both caused and chosen, a key issue in usul al-fiqh when discussing obligation (taklif) and capacity (istita'a).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two meanings of masdar
The first premise explains that verbal nouns (masadir) can refer either to their conceptual meaning (ma'na masdari) or to the external state (hala kharijiyya) resulting from them. This distinction is foundational for later analysis of the act itself.
For example, 'movement' can mean the concept or the actual motion.
2
Understand the necessity of existence from a complete cause
The second premise states that when all conditions for a contingent's existence are present (the complete cause), its existence becomes necessary; if any part is missing, existence is impossible. Follow the objection and response to see how this is defended.
This principle is accepted by both Ahl al-Sunnah and philosophers, but with a key difference.
3
Recognize the role of non-existent relations in causation
The third premise argues that the causes of events must include entities that are neither existent nor non-existent, such as relations (idafat). This is proven by eliminating alternative possibilities (pure existents, pure non-existents, or a mixture).
This sets the stage for understanding choice as a relational attribute.
4
Identify how Ahl al-Sunnah differ from philosophers
Although both accept the necessity of the effect upon the complete cause, Ahl al-Sunnah maintain that this does not entail a necessitating cause without choice, because the cause includes relational elements that leave room for divine and human volition.
This distinction is crucial for the concept of ikhtiyar (choice).

وقد بان لك من هذا التحقيق أن مبدأ الفعل الاختياري يجب أن يكون اضطرارياً، وإلا لزم التسلسل في المبدأ؛ ولصدر الشريعة رحمه الله ها هنا كلام لإثبات الاختيار بمعنى ترجيح أحد الجانبين مع التساوي ومع وجوب المقدمة الثانية : وجود الممكن يجب عند وجود جملة ما يتوقف عليه، وعند عدم شيء منها يمتنع وجوده، أما الأول فلأنه لو لم يجب وجوده أمكن عدمه، فإن توقف وجوده حال العدم على شيء آخر لم تبق العلة التامة علة يقال : قد لزم هذا المعنى فإنه لو لم يجب معها لكان نسبة الوجود والعدم إليه سواء كما كان قبل وجود هذه العلة، فلم يتحقق إيجاد فلزم وجود الممكن من غير موجد مرجح وهو محال، فلا بد من رجحان الوجود على العدم، وترجيح المرجوح محال فالوجود واجب وأما الثاني فلأنه إن لم يمتنع على ذلك التقدير لأمكن وجوده من غير إيجاد علته فلم تبق العلة علة وقال : هذه المقدمة مسلمة أهل السنة والفلاسفة لكن أهل السنة يقولون على وجه لا يلزم منه القول بالعلة الموجبة الغير المختارة بخلاف الفلاسفة .  المقدمة الثالثة : أنه لا بد أن يدخل في علة الحوادث أمور لا موجودة ولا معدومة كالإضافات، وإلا فإما أن تكون علتها موجودات محضة أو معدومات محضة أو مختلطة من الموجودات والمعدومات والشقوق بأسرها باطلة أما الأول فلأنه لو كانت موجودات لا بد لها علة حتى تنتهي إلى الباري جل مجده، فإما يلزم قدم الحوادث أو الاستحالة العظيمة من من ارتفاع يتوقف عليها المعلول الحادث تحقق الحادث، وإلا فيتوقف على عدم آخر، فإما عدم سابق فيلزم قدم الحادث لتحقق جملة ما يتوقف هو عليه من الوجودات المستندة إلى الباري آخراً والعدمات وإما عدم لاحق لشيء وليكن عدم And it has become clear to you from this investigation that the principle of the voluntary act must be involuntary, otherwise infinite regress would occur in the principle. Sadr al-Shariah (may Allah have mercy on him) has here a discussion to establish choice in the sense of preponderating one of two sides despite equality and despite the necessity of the act. So let us mention it and unravel its knot so that the reality becomes clear to you. We say: He (may Allah have mercy on him) first laid down four premises: The first premise: That verbal nouns (masadirs) are sometimes used to mean their source meanings for which they were coined, and sometimes used for the external state resulting from them, like 'movement': it is used to mean its source meaning, and sometimes used for the external state. The first is a conceptual meaning with no external existence except by consideration of the instance, and the second is an objective thing. This is very clear. The second premise: The existence of a contingent becomes necessary upon the existence of the totality of what it depends on, and upon the absence of any part of that totality, its existence becomes impossible. As for the first part: because if its existence were not necessary, it could be absent. If its existence at the time of absence depended on something else, then the complete cause would no longer be a complete cause; and if it did not depend, then its existence sometimes with it and absence at other times would be a preponderance without a preponderator. If it is said: 'Preponderance without a preponderator is impossible, meaning existence of a contingent without an existentiator, and this is not necessary because the existentiator exists there,' I say: This meaning has indeed occurred, because the time of absence did not produce it, and at the time of existence if something produced it, this production would be by what it depends on, so the assumed complete cause would no longer remain; and if nothing produced it, it would necessitate the existence of a contingent without production. Thus he said, and there is something in it. The correct answer is to say: This meaning has indeed occurred, because if it were not necessary with it, the ratio of existence and non-existence would be the same as before the existence of this cause, so no production would be realized, thus the existence of a contingent without a preponderating existentiator would be necessary, which is impossible. Therefore, there must be a preponderance of existence over non-existence, and preponderance of the preponderated is impossible, so existence is necessary. As for the second part: because if it were not impossible on that assumption, then its existence could occur without the production of its cause, so the cause would not be a cause. And he said: This premise is accepted by the Ahl al-Sunnah and the philosophers, but the Ahl al-Sunnah hold it in a way that does not necessitate the doctrine of the necessitating cause that is not chosen, unlike the philosophers. The third premise: That it is necessary that among the causes of events there enter things that are neither existent nor non-existent, like relations (idafat). Otherwise, either their cause consists of purely existent things, or purely non-existent things, or a mixture of existent and non-existent things, and all divisions are false. As for the first: because if they were existents, they would need a cause until terminating to the Exalted Creator, then either the eternity of events would follow or a great impossibility of the Creator being exalted above that. As for the second: because the causality of the non-existent for the existent is inconceivable, and also the components of a composite are among what the composite depends on, so non-existent things cannot be the totality of what it depends on. As for the third: because whenever existences upon which the event depends are realized, the event is realized; otherwise it depends on another non-existence. If it is a preceding non-existence, then the eternity of the event follows due to the realization of the totality of what it depends on of existences ultimately stemming from the Creator and non-existences. If it is a subsequent non-existence of something, say the non-existence of Bakr, then it is not a non-existence of a part of its cause. So that cause, if it is an existent thing, then its non-existence is not a cause unless it is the non-existence of a part of its cause, and so on, leading to great impossibility. And if it is a non-existent thing, it needs...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Sadr al-Shariah's attempt to prove that choice (tarjih) can occur despite equality and despite the necessity of the act. He first establishes four premises; the text covers three fully. The first premise distinguishes between the conceptual meaning of a verbal noun (masdar) and its external instantiation. This is important for understanding that the 'act' as a mental concept is different from the actual event. The second premise is a classic statement on causality: a contingent's existence becomes necessary when its complete cause (al-'illa al-tammah) is present, and impossible if any part is missing. The author defends this against a possible objection, emphasizing that this principle is accepted by Ahl al-Sunnah, but they differ from philosophers in that they do not see the cause as necessitating without choice. The third premise argues that among the causes of events there must be entities that are neither existent nor non-existent, such as relations (idafat). He proves this by eliminating the possibilities that the cause consists of only existents, only non-existents, or a mixture. Each leads to absurdities like the eternity of the world or the impossibility of causation. This premise sets the stage for the concept of 'choice' as a relational attribute. The fourth premise is not fully given, but these three establish the logical foundation for understanding choice within a deterministic framework.

Key Terms
مصدر (masdar): A verbal noun that can signify either its source meaning (conceptual) or the resulting external state
ممكن (mumkin): A contingent being that is neither necessary nor impossible in itself; its existence depends on a cause
علة تامة (al-illa al-tammah): The complete cause whose presence necessitates the existence of the effect, and whose absence causes impossibility
إضافة (idafa): A relation or relative attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent, considered as part of the cause of events
ترجيح (tarjih): Preponderance of one side over another, especially in the context of choice (ikhtiyar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, what happens when all that a contingent depends on (jami' ma yatawaqqaf alayhi) is present?
A) The existence of the contingent becomes impossible.
B) The existence of the contingent becomes necessary.
C) The contingent remains possible.
D) The contingent becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
Sadr al-Shariah's three premises establish that the necessity of an act upon its complete cause does not negate choice, because the cause includes relational, non-material elements like intention and divine will.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas as Revealers of Divine Address
Explains how rulings from non-Quran sources are still divine address

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis consider qiyas a 'muzhir' while Sunnah and Ijma are 'muthbit' and derive the implications for the definition of hukm.

Core Idea
Sunnah and Ijma establish the ruling directly; qiyas reveals it by manifesting the underlying ‘illah.

A critical objection to the Hanafi definition of hukm as divine address is that it excludes rulings from Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas. The Hanafi response is that these sources are kashifah (revealers) of the divine address, not independent legislators. This lesson unpacks the distinction between muthbit (establishing) and muzhir (manifesting) sources, and why qiyas is uniquely called a manifestor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening of the passage: the definition of hukm appears to exclude Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas because they are not directly divine speech. Articulate this objection in your own words.
The objection questions how rulings from these sources can be considered divine address.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The text answers that these sources are 'kashifah' (revealers) of the divine address. What is established by them is ultimately established by the divine address itself. This preserves the definition.
Key term: kashif = revealer.
3
Distinguish muthbit from muzhir
The text explains why Sunnah and Ijma are called muthbit (establishing) while qiyas is called muzhir (manifestor). Qiyas explicitly shows subordination to an original via ‘illah, whereas Sunnah and Ijma need no such intermediary.
Qiyas is 'more explicit in subordination' (أصرح في الفرعية).
4
Reflect on the Quranic wording parallel
The Quranic wording also reveals the intrinsic speech, but it is not called a 'revealer' out of deference. Understand why the signifier is treated as the signified.
This highlights the special status of the Quran.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi framework
Summarize: In Hanafi usul, the ultimate source of all rulings is divine address. Sunnah and Ijma directly establish that ruling; qiyas manifests it through analogical reasoning. Both are valid but epistemologically distinct.
This classification has implications for the authority of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الرابع إنه يخرج من الحد (ما ثبت بالأصول الثلاثة غير الكتاب من السنة والإجماع والقياس لعدم خطاب الله هناك والجواب أنها كاشفة عن الخطاب الإلهي فالثابت بها أي بالأصول الثلاثة (ثابت) (به أي بالخطاب الإلهي، فالحكم حقيقة هو ذلك الخطاب، فإن قلت : فعلى هذا يلزم أن لا يعد نظم القرآن خطاباً، لأنه كاشف أيضاً عن النفسي قال (وأما عدم نظم القرآن منه أي من الكاشف (مع أنه كاشف عن النفسي، فلأن الدال كأنه المدلول) فلا كاشفاً تأدباً، فإن قلت؛ فما بال الحنفية لا ينسبون الكشف إلا إلى القياس؟ قال : (وما عن الحنفية أن القياس مظهر بخلاف السنة والإجماع فمبني على أنه أصرح في الفرعية) فإنه يحتاج إلى أصل مقيس عليه حال أخذ الحكم بخلافهما، إذ لا يحتاج في أخذ الحكم منهما إلى شيء سواهما، فنسبوا إثبات الحكم إليهما، وكشف الحكم إليه فتأمل) فيه فإنه دقيق حقيق بالقبول (ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف فبعضهم جعلوه خطاباً، والآخرون لا، يسمى أن يصير متعلقاته ، والحق أنه خلاف لفظي (إن فسر بما يفهم ولو بالأخرة أي ما فيه صلوح الإفهام (كان خطاباً فيه أي في الأزل، لأنه صالح فيه للإفهام فيما لا يزال (وإن فسر بما أفهم) أي وقع إفهامه (لم يكن في الأزل خطاباً، إذ لم يتحقق الإفهام فيه بل فيما لا يزال فقط، والخطاب في اللغة توجيه الكلام للإفهام، ثم أطلق على الكلام الموجه للإفهام فإن اكتفى بالصلوح للإفادة فالأزلي خطاب في الأزل، وإن أريد الإفهام الحالي فلا، وأما أخذ العلم بإفهامه في الجملة، كما قال السيد قدس سره فغير ظاهر، ولا يفهم من لفظ الخطاب، وما قال في «الحاشية» أن المعتبر في كون الكلام خطاباً أحد الأمرين: الإفهام ،بالفعل أو العلم في الحال بالإفهام في المآل، وأما المفهم بالقوة مع عدم العلم في الحال بكونه مفهماً في المآل فليس إلا خطاباً بالقوة عند الفريقين، فادعاء محض بل الكلام الذي هيىء للإفهام خطاب عند من يكتفي بالصلوح للإفهام في المآل علم أنه يفهم مآلاً أم لا نعم يشترط للعلم بأنه خطاب علم كونه مفهماً، فظهور الخطابية إنما هو بالعلم، وأما نفس الخطابية فبالتهيؤ والتوجه للإفهام ولو مآلاً فتأمل . (ويبتنى عليه أنه حكم في الأزل أو فيما لا يزال فمن قال الكلام خطاب في الأزل قال إنه حكم فيه، ومن لم يقل لا يقول به، فإن قلت كيف يتأتى أزلية الحكم مع أنه الخطاب المتعلق والتعلق حادث؟ قلت؛ المراد بالتعلق في الحد وقوع فعل المكلف من كالمفعول ونحوه، وليس هذا التعلق حادثاً بل الحادث التعلق بمعنى المكلف مشغول الذمة بالفعل وأين هذا من ذاك، كذا في «التحرير»، ولك أن تقول بعبارة أخرى : المراد بالتعلق في الحد التعلق الأعم من التعليقي والتنجيزي والحادث التنجيزي فتدبر.
The fourth investigation: That which is established by the three principles other than the Book—from the Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas—is excluded from the definition due to the absence of Allah's address there. The answer is that they reveal the divine address. So what is established by them is established by the divine address. Thus the ruling in reality is that address. If you say: Accordingly, the wording of the Quran would not be considered address, because it too reveals the intrinsic speech. He said: As for the Quranic wording not being from it (i.e., from the revealer) even though it reveals the intrinsic speech, it is because the signifier is as if the signified, so it is not a revealer, out of deference. If you say: Why do Hanafis attribute revealing only to qiyas? He said: What is narrated from the Hanafis that qiyas is a manifestor (muzhir) unlike Sunnah and Ijma is based on the fact that it is more explicit in subordination. For it needs an analogized original when deriving the ruling, unlike the other two, which need nothing besides themselves for deriving the ruling. So they attributed establishing the ruling to them, and revealing the ruling to it. So reflect, for it is subtle and deserves acceptance. Then regarding calling the speech in pre-eternity an address, there is disagreement: some consider it an address, others do not. The truth is that it is a verbal dispute. If interpreted as 'what is understood, even eventually', i.e., what has the potential for understanding, then it is an address in pre-eternity, because it has the potential for understanding in the future. If interpreted as 'what has actually made understood', then it is not an address in pre-eternity, because understanding did not occur there but only in the future. The address in language is directing speech for understanding; then it was applied to the speech directed for understanding. If one is content with the potential for conveying, then the eternal is an address in pre-eternity. If actual current understanding is intended, then no. As for taking knowledge of its being understood in general, as al-Sayyid said, it is not apparent and is not understood from the word 'address'. And what was said in the gloss that what matters for speech being an address is one of two things: actual understanding or current knowledge of future understanding. As for that which potentially conveys without current knowledge of its being understood in the future, it is only a potential address by both groups. This is a mere claim. Rather, speech prepared for understanding is an address for those who are content with the potential for understanding in the future, whether one knows it will be understood in the future or not. Yes, for knowledge that it is an address, one needs knowledge of its being understood. So the manifestation of being an address is through knowledge, but the essence of being an address is through preparation and direction for understanding, even if in the future. So reflect. And upon this is based whether it is a ruling in pre-eternity or in the future. So whoever says the speech is an address in pre-eternity says it is a ruling there; whoever does not say that does not say it. If you say: How can the ruling be eternal while it is the address that is related, and relation is temporal? I say: What is meant by relation in the definition is the occurrence of the action of the responsible person as an object and the like, and this relation is not temporal; rather, what is temporal is the relation in the sense that the responsible person is obligated with the action. And how far is this from that? Similarly in al-Tahrir. And you may say in other words: What is meant by relation in the definition is the general relation, including both conditional and immediate, and what is temporal is the immediate relation. So ponder.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This is a rule passage. The author addresses the objection that the definition of hukm (address of Allah) excludes rulings derived from Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas since they are not directly from Allah. The response is that these sources are kashifah (revealers) of the divine address; the ruling established by them is ultimately the divine ruling. A subtle point arises: the Quranic wording itself is also a revealer of the intrinsic speech (kalam nafsi), yet it is not called a 'revealer' but rather considered identical in effect to the divine speech out of deference. The author then explains why Hanafis specifically call qiyas a 'muzhir' (manifestor) while Sunnah and Ijma are 'muthbit' (establishers): qiyas is more explicit in subordination, needing an analogical base, whereas Sunnah and Ijma directly establish the ruling without needing another source. The passage then delves into a verbal dispute about whether the eternal speech (kalam azali) can be called 'khitab' (address). The author clarifies that if 'khitab' is defined by the potential for understanding (even future), then it applies eternally; if defined by actual understanding, it only applies temporally. This distinction affects whether the ruling itself is considered eternal or temporal. The relation (ta'alluq) in the definition is general, including both conditional (ta'liqi) and immediate (tanjizi), and only the immediate relation is temporal. Thus the ruling's 'eternity' is preserved.

Key Terms
كاشف (kāshif): A source (like qiyas) that reveals the divine ruling hidden in the primary sources.
مظهر (muzhir): A manifestor; qiyas is called muzhir because it explicitly shows the subordination of the branch to the original via ‘illah.
مثبت (muthbit): An establisher; Sunnah and Ijma directly establish the ruling without needing an analogical base.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, why is qiyas called a 'muzhir' (manifestor) while Sunnah and Ijma are called 'muthbit' (establishing)?
A) Because qiyas is weaker than Sunnah and Ijma
B) Because qiyas explicitly shows the subordination of the branch ruling to an original via 'illah
C) Because qiyas was invented after the time of the Prophet (PBUH)
D) Because qiyas is not a valid source in the Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
Sunnah and Ijma establish rulings directly; qiyas manifests the ruling by revealing its underlying ‘illah, making it a revealer (kashif) of the divine address.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
8 min
Is Eternal Speech an Address? The Verbal Dispute
Analyses the definition of khitab and its implications for the eternity of divine rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two definitions of khitab and explain their impact on the eternity of divine rulings.

Core Idea
Whether divine speech is called ‘address’ in pre-eternity depends on whether we define it by potential or actual understanding.

After clarifying the role of Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas as revealers, the text addresses a subtle terminological dispute: is God’s eternal speech (kalam azali) considered an 'address' (khitab)? The answer hinges on the definition of khitab. This lesson unpacks the two definitions and shows how this dispute affects whether the ruling (hukm) is considered eternal or temporal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of khitab
The passage presents two interpretations: 'what is understood, even eventually' (potential) and 'what has actually made understood' (actual). Write them down.
The linguistic origin of khitab is 'directing speech for understanding'.
2
Apply each definition to eternal speech
Under the potential definition, eternal speech qualifies as khitab because it has the potential to be understood in the future. Under the actual definition, it does not, because understanding occurs in time.
The author calls this a 'verbal dispute' (khilaf lafzi).
3
Understand the implication for the ruling’s eternity
The text states that upon this dispute depends whether the ruling is considered in pre-eternity or only later. If khitab is eternal, the ruling is eternal; if not, the ruling is temporal.
Key concept: ta‘alluq (relation) – the general relation includes both conditional (ta‘liqi) and immediate (tanjizi).
4
Resolve the dispute according to the author
The author leans toward the potential definition, noting that speech prepared for understanding is address. The eternal speech is such, whether or not we know it will be understood.
The manifestation of being an address is through knowledge, but its essence is through preparation.
5
Connect to the broader usul framework
This discussion reinforces that the divine address (and thus hukm) is eternal in essence, while its temporal effect (obligation) occurs at the time of revelation.
This aligns with the Hanafi doctrine of kalam nafsi.
