The Hanafi Rule of Majaz: Cause for Effect
Defends the validity of using cause for effect figuratively in legal terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that permits using the word for a cause to refer to its effect (sabab → musabbab) and apply it to interpret legal expressions.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit calling the effect by its cause, but not the reverse.

This lesson explores a key rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the permissibility of figuratively using a term for a cause (sabab) to refer to its effect (musabbab). Grounded in repeated need and causal relation, this rule allows jurists to interpret phrases like 'you are free' as effecting divorce in slave-wife contexts. We will examine the examples, the Shafi'i objection, and the Hanafi response, then consider how the rule is applied and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its condition
Extract the core rule: figurative usage of cause for effect is valid, but only when there is repeated need (lā tataqara). Note that the opposite direction (effect for cause) is not allowed.
2
Classify the examples
Examine the examples: using 'manumission' for 'divorce', and using 'sale' or 'gift' for 'marriage'. Determine how each illustrates the rule—the cause (itq, bay', hiba) leads to the same effect (removal or establishment of marital rights).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Understand al-Shafi'i's argument: that such figurative usage is exclusive to the Prophet (ṣalla llāhu ʿalayhi wa-sallam) and cannot be generalized. Identify the proof he adduces (the verse about the gift of self).
4
Examine the Hanafi response
Study the Hanafi rebuttal: the exclusivity (ikhlāṣ) refers to the negation of dowry, not to the metaphorical usage. Also, the examples are either causes for the same effect or metaphors based on resemblance.
5
Generalize the principle
Recognize that the given examples are not strict cause-effect (e.g., sale and marriage are both causes of ownership), so the rule applies by generalizing causality or by metaphor. This shows the principle's flexibility in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح تجوز السبب للمسبب) فيما لا تكرر للافتقار (فيصح العتق (للطلاق فإن العتق إزالة ملك الرقبة وهي سبب لإزالة ملك المتعة، كما في الأمة (و) يصح (البيع والهبة للنكاح فإنهما لإثبات ملك الرقبة، وهو سبب لإثبات ملك المتعة خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى (فيهما) أي في الهبة والبيع، لا لأجل الفساد في التجوز من السبب إلى المسبب، بل لأن هذا الانعقاد عنده من خصائص الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه وأهل بيته وبارك وسلم بالنص خالصة لك، ونحن نقول : إن الخلوص راجع إلى نفي المهر، كما يكون في الهبة الحقيقية، فالمعنى والله أعلم أنا أحللنا امرأة مؤمنة وهبت نفسها للنبي أي من غير ،بدل وأراد النبي أن ينكحها حال كون الواهبة خالصة لك أيها النبي، فإنها لغيرك لا تحل من غير بدل، أو راجعة إلى مطلق أزواجه المطهرات أي أحللنا أزواجك حال كونهن خالصة لك من دون المؤمنين، فإنهن أمهاتهم لا تحل لهم ، وأما تخصيص المجاز لشأن النبي الله كما يقوله الشافعي رضي الله عنه فمما لا وجه له فتدبر، ثم إن كون هذه الأمثلة مما نحن فيه غير ظاهر، لأن الهبة والبيع عقدان مخصوصان سببان لملك الرقبة والنكاح عقد مخصوص سبب لملك المتعة وليس أحدهما سبباً للآخر، بل هما سببا شيء واحد، فإن النكاح سبب لملك المتعة والبيع والهبة أيضاً سبباه، إلا أن النكاح بالذات وهما بالعرض، وكذلك الإعتاق تصرف من المعتق في المملوك يوجب العتق والتطليق تصرف من الزوج في الزوجة يوجب زوال ملك المتعة، فليس أحدهما سبباً للآخر، فهذه الأمثلة خارجة عما نحن فيه من إطلاق السبب على المسبب فلا مخلص، إلا أن تعمم السببية بأن يكون سبباً له أو سبباً لما وضع ذلك الشيء له، فالبيع والهبة سببان لما وضع النكاح له، وكذا العتاق سبب لما وضع الطلاق له وهو زوال ملك المتعة، هذا ويمكن أن تجعل هذه الأمثلة استعارات فاستعمل البيع والهبة في النكاح لمشابهة النكاح إياهما في إفادة ملك المتعة، ولا يجوز العكس أي استعمال النكاح فيهما لعدم إفادة النكاح ملك الرقبة المفاد بهما، هذا والطلاق أيضاً مشابه للعتق في إزالة ملك المتعة وأما العكس فسيجيء حاله مشروحاً إن شاء الله تعالى.
And the figurative usage of the cause for the effect is valid) in what is not repeated due to need. (So itq (manumission) is valid for talaq (divorce), for manumission is removal of the ownership of the neck, which is a cause for removal of ownership of the marriage pleasure, as in the case of a slave-woman. (And) sale and gift are valid for marriage, for they are for establishing ownership of the neck, which is a cause for establishing ownership of pleasure, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (In them) i.e., gift and sale, not due to corruption in figurative usage from cause to effect, but because this conclusion in his view is from the special characteristics of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions)... and we say: the exclusivity refers to the negation of the dowry, as in a real gift. So the meaning, Allah knows best, is that We have permitted a believing woman who gave herself to the Prophet, i.e., without exchange, and the Prophet wanted to marry her while she was exclusive to you, O Prophet, for for other than you she is not lawful without exchange. Or it refers to his pure wives, i.e., We have permitted your wives while they are exclusive to you apart from the believers, for they are their mothers, not lawful for them. As for the specification of majaz for the Prophet's affair as al-Shafi'i says, it has no basis, so reflect. Then, these examples not being apparent in our discussion, because gift and sale are specific contracts that are causes for ownership of the neck, and marriage is a specific contract that is a cause for ownership of pleasure, and neither is a cause for the other, rather they are both causes for the same thing: marriage is a cause for ownership of pleasure, and sale and gift are also causes for it, except that marriage is essential and they are accidental. Similarly, manumission is an act of the manumitter on the owned slave that necessitates freedom, and divorce is an act of the husband on the wife that necessitates the removal of ownership of pleasure, so neither is a cause for the other. So these examples are outside our discussion of using cause for effect, so there is no escape, unless we generalize causality such that it is a cause for it or a cause for what that thing is placed for. So sale and gift are causes for what marriage is placed for, and likewise manumission is a cause for what divorce is placed for, which is removal of ownership of pleasure. And it is possible to make these examples as metaphors, so sale and gift are used for marriage due to the similarity of marriage to them in conveying ownership of pleasure, and the opposite is not allowed, i.e., using marriage for them, because marriage does not convey ownership of the neck conveyed by them. And similarly, divorce is similar to manumission in removing ownership of pleasure; as for the opposite, its situation will come explained, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage establishes the rule that figurative usage from cause (sabab) to effect (musabbab) is valid in shariah, provided there is repeated need. Examples: using 'manumission' (itq) for 'divorce' (talaq) because manumission removes ownership of the slave, which is a cause for removing ownership of marital pleasure (as with a slave-wife). Similarly, using 'sale' and 'gift' for 'marriage' because they establish ownership of property, which is a cause for establishing marital pleasure. The author then refutes al-Shafi'i's objection that these are sunnah-specific. He clarifies that these examples are not strictly cause-effect but rather both are causes for the same result, so the analogy holds by generalization. Alternatively, they can be understood as metaphors based on resemblance. The discussion is nuanced for advanced students.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause or means that leads to a legal effect.
مسبب (musabbab): The effect or result caused by a legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is an example of valid figurative usage from cause to effect?
A) Using 'marriage' to mean 'sale'.
B) Using 'manumission' to mean 'divorce'.
C) Using 'divorce' to mean 'manumission'.
D) Using 'gift' to mean 'divorce'.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul permits using the word for a cause to refer to its effect, provided there is repeated need, but prohibits the reverse.

Legal Utterances as External Causes (al-Asbab al-Kharijiyya)
Usul rule: utterances as external causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi concept of external causes in legal utterances and distinguish it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Legal utterances are real external causes that necessitate rulings, not mere signs.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the words we use in contracts and divorce are not just linguistic expressions; they are efficient causes that bring about legal changes. This lesson explores the nature of these utterances as external causes, drawing an analogy with physical causality. Understanding this principle is key to appreciating how the Hanafi school derives rulings from language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the passage and identify the central thesis: legal formulas are external causes (asbab kharijiyya) for rulings.
The analogy with killing causing death is crucial.
2
Analyze the analogy
Compare the causality of killing (physical cause) with the causality of utterances (legal cause). What is the common element?
Both produce an effect directly in the external world.
3
Understand the intended effect
The passage states that the intended effect is the external ruling (e.g., divorce), not the linguistic meaning. Clarify this distinction.
The speaker intends the legal consequence, not to convey a meaning.
4
Contrast with alternative views
Consider how this differs from the Shafi'i view that causes are mere signs (amarat) that indicate rulings. Why does it matter?
The Hanafi view emphasizes the direct causal link.
5
Apply to a specific case
Apply the principle to a classic Hanafi example: the utterance 'You are divorced' spoken in jest. Does the ruling occur?
In Hanafi fiqh, divorce in jest is effective because the cause (utterance) is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثانية قيل هذه الألفاظ أي الصيغ الشرعية من العقود والفسوخ (أسباب خارجية) للأحكام على مثال سببية القتل للموت في الخارج، وإنما يقصد وقوع الحكم (المقصود بالإنشاء منها بطريق قصد أثر مخصوص خارجي عن سبب خارجي) لا بطريق أن يقصد معانيها المؤثرة في الأحكام فهذه الألفاظ ليس لها معنى) يعتبر عند الإيقاع بل أنفسها معان خارجية
Second benefit: It is said that these terms – the legal formulas of contracts and annulments – are external causes for legal rulings, on the pattern of the causality of killing for death in the external world. Indeed, what is intended by their construction is the occurrence of the ruling, through the intention of a specific external effect from an external cause, not through intending their meanings that affect the rulings. So these terms have no meaning considered at the time of utterance; rather, they themselves are external meanings.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage explains that utterances used in contracts and annulments (like 'I sell', 'you are divorced') are external causes (asbab kharijiyya) for legal effects. Just as killing in the real world causes death, these words cause legal changes (e.g., transfer of ownership, dissolution of marriage). The speaker's intention is not directed towards the linguistic meanings of the words but toward the external effect (e.g., actual divorce). Thus, these utterances are not considered as having inner meanings that affect rulings; rather, they themselves are the external causes. This aligns with the Hanafi view in usul al-fiqh that the lafz in such contexts are effective as causes (asbab) and the intention behind them is to produce the legal result, not to convey linguistic meaning.

Key Terms
السبب الخارجي (al-sabab al-khariji): An external cause that gives rise to a legal ruling, such as utterances in contracts, similar to physical causes like killing causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second benefit, what is the nature of legal formulas like 'You are divorced'?
A) They are linguistic expressions with independent meanings
B) They are external causes that produce legal effects
C) They have no effect on the ruling
D) They are only considered if written

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, legal utterances are real external causes that directly produce legal rulings, analogous to physical causality.

Hanafi Interpretation of 'Aw' in the Muharaba Verse
Defending Hanafi gradation in muharaba punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of contextual limitation (takhsis bi al-qara'in) to interpret Quranic 'amm' and derive proportional punishments.

Core Idea
Hanafi school limits 'aw' generality in muharaba via contextual evidence, ensuring proportional justice.

The muharaba verse (Quran 5:33) lists four punishments connected by 'aw' (or). Opponents argue this gives the Imam unrestricted choice. The Hanafi school, however, holds that contextual evidence (qara'in) from prophetic practice, logical proportionality, and the verse's own structure restricts the Imam's discretion. In this lesson, we will examine the Hanafi istidlal and see how they reconcile the apparent generality of 'aw' with the need for fitting punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opposing View and Its Dalil
State the position that 'aw' indicates full discretion for the Imam. Recognize that this relies on the apparent generality (zahir al-umum) of the particle 'aw' in connecting the punishments.
Opponents include 'Ata', Sa'id, Mujahid, and others; they hold the verse gives the Imam free choice among all penalties.
2
State the Hanafi Position
Explain that Hanafis tie each punishment to a specific crime type: execution for killing, crucifixion for killing+robbery, amputation for robbery, banishment for mere intimidation. In combined crimes, a limited choice exists (e.g., crucifixion or amputation+execution), but never the lightest for the worst or vice versa.
Abu Hanifa's view: for killing+robbery, Imam chooses among crucifixion, amputation+crucifixion, or amputation+execution – not banishment.
3
Trace the Hanafi Response to the Opposing Dalil
The Hanafi response argues that accepting full discretion leads to absurd consequences (crucifying a mere intimidator or banishing a murderer), violating legal proportionality (qiyas al-munasaba). Thus, the generality of 'aw' is restricted (takhsis) by contextual evidence from the verse's own gradation and from the Sunna.
This is a classic case of takhsis al-amm bi al-qara'in (limiting a general text by indicators).
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference is clear: under the opposing view, a murderer could be banished; under the Hanafi view, a murderer must be executed or crucified, never merely banished. This demonstrates how a seemingly minor textual interpretation yields vastly different legal outcomes.
Understanding this thamarah is crucial for fatwa and judicial practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادًا أَن يُقَتَّلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَّبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُم مِّنْ خِلَافٍ أَوْ يُنفَوْا مِن الأَرْضِ﴾ [المائدة: 33] وهو يقتضي أن يكون الإمام مخيراً في جميع المارة، كما هو مذهب عطاء وسعيد بن المسيب ومجاهد والضحاك والنخعي وأبي ثور وداود الظاهري، ونقل في كتبنا عن مالك، وأنتم لا تقولون به بل مذهبكم جزاؤهم القتل إن كانوا قتلوا، والصلب إن قتلوا وأخذوا المال والقطع إن أخذوا المال فقط، والنفي أي الحبس الدائم إن خوفوا من غير أخذ وقتل : بل أبو حنيفة الإمام رحمه الله يقول في القتل والأخذ يخير الإمام بين أن يصلب فقط، أو يقطع ويصلب، أو يقطع ويقتل . قال : (وفي آية المحاربة يلزم) من تخيير الإمام بين الأجزية (مقابلة أخف الجنايات بالأغلظ من الأجزية فإنه يجوز له حينئذ أن يصلب إن خوّف فقط (وبالعكس) أي مقابلة أغلظ الجنايات بأخف الأجزية، كما إذا قتل
If it is said: Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed the recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive upon earth [to cause] corruption is that they be killed or crucified or that their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides or that they be banished from the land.' [Al-Ma'idah 5:33] This implies that the Imam is given choice among all punishments, as is the madhhab of 'Ata, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, Mujahid, al-Dahhak, al-Nakha'i, Abu Thawr, and Dawud al-Zahiri, and it is transmitted from Malik in our books. But you do not hold this; rather your madhhab is that their punishment is execution if they killed, crucifixion if they killed and took wealth, amputation if they took wealth only, and banishment i.e., permanent imprisonment if they terrorized without taking or killing. Rather, Abu Hanifa, the Imam, says regarding killing and taking: the Imam chooses between either crucifying only, or amputating and crucifying, or amputating and executing. He said: In the verse of muharaba, the choice of the Imam among punishments necessitates matching the lightest crimes with the harshest punishments, for it would then be permissible to crucify for mere terrorizing, and conversely, matching the harshest crimes with the lightest punishments, as when one kills.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute about the interpretation of the muharaba verse (Quran 5:33). The opponents (various scholars and madhhabs) argue that the particle 'aw' (or) indicates that the Imam has free choice among the four punishments for all muharaba crimes. The Hanafi position, represented by Abu Hanifa, does not accept free choice. Instead, it distinguishes the punishment based on the specific crime: execution for murder only, crucifixion for murder and robbery, amputation for robbery only, and banishment for spreading fear without property or murder. Additionally, in cases involving both murder and robbery, the Imam has a limited choice among crucifixion, amputation plus crucifixion, or amputation plus execution, but not the lighter punishments. The author criticizes the opposing view because it would allow absurd consequences like crucifying a mere intimidator or banishing a murderer, which violates proportionality. The passage defends the Hanafi position by showing that the literal generality of 'aw' is overridden by contextual evidence (qara'in) such as the gravity of the crime.

Key Terms
مُحَارَبَة (muḥāraba): Armed rebellion or highway robbery that involves killing, taking property, or spreading fear.
تَخْيِير (takhyeer): Choice given to the Imam among certain punishments in specific cases; not absolute.
أَوْ (aw): Disjunctive particle 'or'; in Quranic context indicates alternatives.
قَرَائِن (qara'in): Contextual indicators that specify or limit the meaning of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi reading of Quran 5:33, what is the punishment for a muharaba criminal who killed multiple people but did not take property?
A) The Imam may choose execution, crucifixion, or amputation at his discretion.
B) The criminal must be executed; other options are not allowed.
C) The criminal must be crucified because killing requires crucifixion.
D) The criminal must be banished because no property was taken.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi limitation of 'aw' in muharaba exemplifies how contextual evidence (qara'in) restricts the apparent generality of a Quranic text to ensure proportionate justice.

The Linguistic Basis of Price in Hanafi Sales: Bā' al-Muqābala as Bā' al-Isti'āna
Applying the meaning of باء المقابلة to a sale transaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of bā' used in a sale contract and apply the Hanafi rule to determine the permissibility of substitution before possession.

Core Idea
The باء of price is a باء of seeking help, making the price a means; substitution is valid only when the باء enters upon the price.

This lesson explores a subtle linguistic point in usul al-fiqh: the classification of the preposition bā' in sales contracts. The Hanafi school argues that the bā' that enters upon the price (thaman) is a bā' al-isti'āna (seeking help), not a bā' of substitution. This classification directly impacts the ruling on substituting goods before taking possession—a key issue in the law of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic dispute
Recognize that the bā' in a price phrase can be interpreted either as bā' al-muqābala (exchange) or bā' al-isti'āna (seeking help). The Hanafi position is that it is a type of bā' al-isti'āna because the price is a means to obtain the object.
The objector might see bā' al-muqābala as bā' al-badaliyya (substitution), giving equal weight to price and object.
2
Understand the 'illa (effective cause)
The 'illa for treating the bā' as bā' al-isti'āna is that prices (athmān) are fundamentally means (wasā'il) to acquire goods. They are established as debts in the obligation (thābitat 'alā al-dhimma), reinforcing their instrumental nature.
This reasoning is talīl: it shows why the bā' functions like the bā' of seeking help.
3
Apply to the first case
In 'I bought this slave for a kurr of described wheat', the kurr is the price, and the bā' (which enters upon it) is bā' al-isti'āna. Therefore, substitution before possession is valid because the kurr is a price, not the sold item.
Substitution (istibdāl) means exchanging the price for something else before taking possession.
4
Apply to the reverse case
In 'I bought a kurr of described wheat for this slave', the kurr is now the sold object (because the bā' enters upon 'this slave'). This creates a salam contract (forward sale), which requires deferred delivery and strict rules—including the prohibition of substitution before possession.
Salam has its own conditions (e.g., specification of time). The bā' here is also bā' al-isti'āna, but it enters upon the price (the slave), making the transaction salam.
5
Defend against the objection
The objector claims the bā' is bā' al-badaliyya (substitution). The Hanafi response is based on the fundamental purpose of prices: they are placed to be a means, not an end. This is consistent even if some individuals intend the price as an end—the general placement (waḍ') governs the linguistic rule.
The objection is repelled by sticking to the original waḍ' of prices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباء المقابلة التي تدخل الأثمان أشبه بالاستعانة بل نوع منها فإن الأثمان وسائل يستعان بها على المقاصد وهي المبيعات، وربما يقال: إن المبيع كما يكون مقصوداً عند المشتري، كذلك الثمن يكون مقصوداً عند البائع، فلا وجه لجعل باء المقابلة باء الاستعانة والحق غير خفي على ذي بصيرة، فإن المقصود إن الأثمان إنما وضعت لأن تجعل وسيلة إلى تحصيل شيء، ولهذا تثبت على الذمة، وهذا لا ينافي كونها مقصودة عند البعض، وإذا كان وضع الأثمان لذلك التزم استعمال باء الاستعانة داخلة عليها، وإذا أثبت أن الأثمان تكون مدخولة الباء فصح الاستبدال بالبيع ونحوه بالكرّ من الحنطة قبل القبض في اشتريت هذا العبد بكرّ حنطة موصوفة فإنه ثمن لدخول باء المقابلة والاستعانة عليه والاستبدال فيه قبل القبض جائز دون العكس أي لا يجوز الاستبدال فيما إذا قال: اشتريت كراً من حنطة موصوفة بهذا العبد لأنه سلم حينئذ لكون الكر الذي في الذمة مبيعاً لتعلق الشراء به، وهذا العبد ثمناً لدخول الباء عليه وهو حقيقة السلم وإذا كان سلماً فلا بد من شروطه من الأجل وغيره ولا بد فيه من القبض أيضاً فلا يصح الاستبدال قبل القبض، هذا وقرر في «التحرير» هذه الفريعة على أن الباء باء الاستعانة ومدخوله يكون ثمناً، وكان أورد عليه أن هذه الباء باء البدلية ولا نسلم كونها باء الاستعانة، وقررها المصنف بحيث اندفع هذا السؤال
And the باء of exchange (al-muqābala) that enters upon the price is similar to the باء of seeking help, rather a type of it. For prices are means by which one seeks help for purposes, and they are the sold items. Some may say: Just as the sold item is intended by the buyer, so also the price is intended by the seller, so there is no reason to make the باء of exchange a باء of seeking help. But the truth is not hidden from a discerning person: the intention is that prices are only placed to be a means to obtain something, hence they are established in the obligation. This does not negate that they are intended by some. Since the placement of prices is like that, the use of the باء of seeking help is consistently applied to them. Once it is established that prices are entered by the باء, then substitution (al-istibdāl) is valid in a sale and the like by a kurr of wheat before possession in 'I bought this slave for a kurr of wheat described' because it is a price due to the entry of the باء of exchange and seeking help upon it, and substitution in it before possession is permissible. But not the reverse, i.e., substitution is not permissible when he says: 'I bought a kurr of described wheat for this slave' because then it is a salam (sale with deferred delivery) since the kurr in obligation is the sold item because the purchase is attached to it, and this slave is the price due to the entry of the باء upon it, and it is the reality of salam. When it is a salam, its conditions (like deferred time) are necessary, and also possession is necessary, so substitution before possession is not valid. This, and it was established in 'al-Tahrir' this subsidiary issue that the باء is the باء of seeking help and its object is the price. It was objected that this باء is the باء of substitution, and we do not accept it being the باء of seeking help. But the author established it such that this objection is repelled.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage discusses the باء of exchange (باء المقابلة) that enters upon the price in sale contracts. The author argues that this باء is actually a type of باء الاستعانة (seeking help) because the price is a means to obtain the sold item. He then applies this to a specific fiqhi issue: if someone says 'I bought this slave for a kurr of described wheat', the kurr is the price (due to باء المقابلة), and substitution before possession is allowed. Conversely, if he says 'I bought a kurr of described wheat for this slave', then it is a salam contract (forward sale) because the kurr is the sold item and the slave is the price; substitution before possession is not valid in salam. This ruling is from Hanafi fiqh and relies on the linguistic analysis of the باء.

Key Terms
باء المقابلة (bāʼ al-muqābala): The باء of exchange, used for prices.
الاستبدال (al-istibdāl): Substitution of goods before possession; valid in certain sales.
باء الاستعانة (bāʼ al-istiʻāna): The باء of seeking help; indicates a means.
باء البدلية (bāʼ al-badaliyya): The باء of substitution; indicates equal exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which of the following sentences is substitution before possession valid?
A) 'I bought this slave for a kurr of described wheat'
B) 'I bought a kurr of described wheat for this slave'
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of the باء in a price phrase as باء الاستعانة determines the validity of substitution before possession, distinguishing between a price (thaman) and a sold object (mabīʿ).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
Generality of a Ruling with a Cause: By Qiyas or By Wording?
Determining whether a ruling motivated by a cause becomes general to all instances of that cause by qiyas or by wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a cause-statement yields generality by qiyas (not wording) and distinguish between independent (mustaqilla) and corrective (musahhiha) causes.

Core Idea
A ruling motivated by an independent cause applies by qiyas to all instances; wording alone does not make it general.

Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates. Does this mean all intoxicants are prohibited by the same statement? The Hanafi school says yes, but by analogy (qiyas), not because the wording itself is general. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between independent and corrective causes, a foundational tool for extending rulings in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views: by qiyas (Hanafi/Shafi'i), by wording (al-Nazzam), by additional evidence (al-Baqillani). This sets the stage for the debate.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand the argument: the cause is independent (mustaqilla); wherever it is found, the ruling is found. This is the core of the Hanafi position.
The cause is deemed stipulative (ja'li) and transitive.
3
Examine the counterexample
The freeing example ('I freed Zayd because of his blackness') is used to argue against wording generality. The Hanafi response distinguishes blackness as a corrective cause, not independent.
A corrective cause (musahhiha) only validates the action, not necessitating it.
4
Differentiate cause types
Independent cause (intoxication) necessitates the ruling; corrective cause (blackness) only validates the action. This is key to understanding why the freeing example does not refute the Hanafi view.
5
Apply to Shari'ah
The Lawgiver's causes are stipulative and transitive; thus, qiyas is valid. The ruling extends to all instances of the cause by analogy, not by the wording itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (إذا علل الشارع حكماً بعلة بأن يقول : الخمر حرام لأنه مسكر عم في محالها) أي فيما يوجد فيه تلك العلة (بالقياس) لا بالصيغة قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية: نص عليه الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه (وقيل) عم (بالصيغة) وعليه النظام (و) قال القاضي أبو بكر) الباقلاني أصلا لا بالقياس ولا بالصيغة، بل يحتاج إلى دليل زائد كالمناسبة وتنقيح المناط وغيرهما لنا الظاهر استقلال العلة بالعلية وكلما وجدت العلة المستقلة دخل للاستقلال والاستلزام فيلزم العتق ضرورة أنه صدر من أهل الإعتاق صيغة دالة عليه وضعاً أو عرفاً، قال في الحاشية الكلام في العلل الجعلية لا الواقعية فحينئذ هما سواء، فتأمل ففيه كلام بعد، ووجه بأن الخطابات الإلهية وإن كانت معللة بالعلل الجعلية، لكنها متضمنة لأحكام متعدية فإن الشارع إنما وضع العلة علة لتحقق الحكم أينما وجدت تلك العلة، فالعلة ملزومة للحكم بخلاف هذه العلة لأنها ليست علة للحكم حتى تستلزمه، بل لصحة الإعتاق، فلا يلزم منه إلا صلوحه للإعتاق لا تحققه هذا والحق ما قررناه ولا يرد عليه شيء فتدبر . قال القاضي: يحتمل أن يكون خصوصية المحل جزءاً منها فلا يتجاوز الحكم غيرها (قلنا) أولاً : المسكر ( في أصل عموم الحكم فإن الحكم فيهما
Issue: When the Lawgiver motivates a ruling with a cause by saying, for example: 'Wine is prohibited because it is intoxicating,' it becomes general in its loci (i.e., wherever that cause is found) by qiyas (analogy), not by the wording. Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) stated it. And it was said: it becomes general by the wording, and upon this is al-Nazzam. And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: fundamentally neither by qiyas nor by wording, but rather a further evidence is needed, such as munasaba (suitability) and tanqih al-manat (refinement of the basis) and others. Our proof: it is apparent that the cause is independent in causality, and whenever the independent cause is found, the effect is found, so generality follows in the loci of the cause. And this generality is not by the wording; otherwise, if one said: 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness,' it would necessitate freeing all black slaves, because it would then be like saying 'I freed every black person,' and the consequence is invalid by agreement, so the premise is likewise. If you said: This would also follow if the generality were by qiyas, for it would be like 'I freed every black person' in the generality of the ruling due to the apparent independence of the cause as a cause. I say: No, because qiyas is not made in the Shari'ah a cause for freeing or divorcing; rather, a wording indicating that by conventional or customary indication is necessary, so it does not follow on the assumption of no generality by the wording, as is not hidden for the servant of fiqh principles. I say: There is reflection on this, by denying the entailment, because blackness is a corrective cause for freeing, not necessitating it necessarily, unlike intoxication, for it is an independent cause, so the generality in the necessary cause does not necessitate generality in the corrective cause. So reflect, for it is not complete, because if generality were by the wording, it would be by the particle 'li' or its equivalent indicating generality, and independence and entailment have no role; thus, freeing would be necessary because it issued from a person of freeing a wording indicating it conventionally or customarily. He said in the gloss: The discussion is about stipulative causes, not real ones; then they are equal. So reflect, for there is further speech. And it is justified that the divine addresses, though motivated by stipulative causes, still contain transitive rulings; for the Lawgiver only placed the cause as a cause for the realization of the ruling wherever that cause is found. So the cause is entailed by the ruling, unlike this cause (blackness), for it is not a cause for the ruling to entail it, but rather for the validity of freeing; so nothing follows from it except suitability for freeing, not realization. This, and the truth is what we have established, and nothing refutes it, so contemplate. The Qadi said: It is possible that the specificity of the locus is part of it, so the ruling does not go beyond it. We say: Firstly, this probability is weak, so it is not considered; secondly, it necessitates the invalidity of qiyas absolutely. Those who generalize by the wording said: 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates' is like 'Alcohol is prohibited', and the second is general by wording, so is the first. We said: 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates' is like 'Alcohol is prohibited' in the foundation of generality of ruling, for the ruling in both...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule: when the Lawgiver (الشارع) states a ruling along with a cause (علة), does the ruling apply to all instances of that cause? The text presents three positions: (1) Imam Shafi'i and the Hanafi author hold that it becomes general by qiyas (analogy), not by the wording itself. (2) Some (al-Nazzam) hold it becomes general by the wording (الصيغة). (3) Al-Baqillani holds that it requires additional evidence. The author defends the first position: the cause is independent (مستقلة) and wherever it exists, the ruling exists. He distinguishes between an independent cause (like intoxication) which entails the ruling, and a corrective cause (like blackness in 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness') which only makes the action valid (مصححة) but does not necessitate the ruling. A counterargument that this would also apply to the wording case is refuted by noting that in the Shari'ah, qiyas is not a cause for freeing or divorce; a specific wording is needed. The gloss further clarifies that we are dealing with stipulative (جعلي) causes, not real ones, and that divine addresses are transitive. The passage ends with a rebuttal of the Qadi's possibility that the specificity of the locus might be part of the cause.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): A cause or rationale (sabab) given by the Lawgiver for a ruling, e.g., intoxication as the cause for prohibition of wine.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared cause.
صيغة (ṣīgha): The wording or linguistic form itself that indicates generality.
علة مستقلة ('illa mustaqilla): An independent cause that necessitates the ruling wherever it is found.
علة مصححة ('illa muṣaḥḥiḥa): A corrective cause that only makes an action valid for a ruling but does not necessitate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, how does a motivated ruling (e.g., 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates') become general to all intoxicants?
A) By the wording (الصيغة) because the cause is mentioned.
B) By qiyas (القياس) because the cause is independent and entails the ruling wherever found.
C) It does not become general at all; each intoxicant needs separate textual evidence.
D) By ijma' (consensus) of the scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that when the Lawgiver gives a cause for a ruling, the ruling applies to all instances of that cause by qiyas, not by the wording of the cause-statement.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Core Dispute: Generality by Qiyas or Wording
Dispute over the mechanism of generality from a legislative cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on how a shari‘i ‘illa generates generality and apply the Hanafi argument that generality is by qiyas (analogy), not by wording.

Core Idea
When the Lawgiver gives a cause, the ruling extends to all instances by qiyas, not by the wording itself.

This passage opens a classical usul dispute: does a legislative cause ('illa) like 'because it is intoxicating' generalize its ruling by its own wording or by analogical reasoning? The Hanafi school defends the latter view, and the text presents the core proof and initial rebuttals. We will unpack the three schools' positions and the Hanafi argument that wording would lead to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's core question
Recognize that the issue is how a ta'lil (e.g., 'Wine is haram because it is intoxicating') generates generality (umum) for other cases sharing the same cause. Is it by the wording of the statement itself (sigha) or by qiyas?
2
List the three positions
From the text: (1) Imam al-Shafi‘i and the Hanafis: generality is by qiyas, not wording. (2) Al-Nazzam: by wording. (3) Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: neither, needs additional evidence like munasaba.
The text attributes the qiyas position to the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi‘i.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (lana)
The Hanafi proof: the ‘illa is independent in causality; wherever it exists, the ruling exists, so generality follows by qiyas. But if it were by wording, then saying 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness' would imply freeing all black slaves, which is false by consensus.
This proof uses a counterexample (blackness) to show wording cannot be the vehicle of generality.
4
Consider the objection that qiyas also leads to the same absurdity
A hypothetical opponent argues: if the cause is independent, then even on qiyas view, the blackness example would generalize. The text's reply: qiyas itself is not a legal cause for transactions like emancipation; a verbal or customary indicator is still required for the effect to occur.
This rebuttal preserves the distinction between causal independence and legal effect.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The Hanafi view holds that the wording of a ta'lil does not have conventional or customary generality. Instead, it identifies the cause, and then qiyas (which is a valid source) extends the ruling to all loci of that cause.
The practical result: 'Wine is haram because it intoxicates' makes all intoxicants haram via qiyas, not because the phrase 'because it intoxicates' is itself a general statement.

الخطابات الإلهية وإن كانت معللة بالعلل الجعلية، لكنها متضمنة لأحكام متعدية فإن الشارع إنما وضع العلة علة لتحقق الحكم أينما وجدت تلك العلة، فالعلة ملزومة للحكم بخلاف هذه العلة لأنها ليست علة للحكم حتى في كونه لفظاً متصفاً بالعموم ، فهو إذن بالصيغة (فتأمل) فإنه لا يتم، إذ يجوز أن يكون الغرض بيان نفس علة الحكم لا الاستدلال على الحكم حتى يحتاج إلى الكبرى الكلية، هذا محصل «الحاشية»، قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: القائل بالعموم بالصيغة لا يقول إن صيغة العلة دالة على العموم مطابقة، فهو باطل قطعاً ولا يليق بحال عاقل أن يريده، ولعل مقصوده، أن التعليل يقتضي ثبوت الحكم عموماً بطريق دلالة النص، ولذا قال به من نفى القياس، فلا يستعان بالقياس هذا وهذا كلام متين لكنه يجب أن ينظر في أنه إن أريد أن هيئة هذا التركيب من الاقتران بحرف التعليل موضوعة لغة أو عرفاً لتعميم الحكم، فهو أيضاً باطل، وإلا لزم في أعتقته عن الحنفية والإمام أحمد، ويختاره أن التنصيص بالعلة يوجب الحكم في الفرع من دون توقف على شرع القياس لجلاء الأمر فيه فانظره فإن النزاع لفظي .
Issue: When the Lawgiver conditions a ruling with a cause by saying: 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating', it gives generality to its loci (i.e., wherever that cause exists) by analogy (qiyas), not by the wording. The author of Matali‘ al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: Imam al-Shafi‘i (may Allah be pleased with him) stated this. And it was said: (it gives generality) by the wording, and al-Nazzam held this. And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: originally neither by analogy nor by wording, but needs an additional evidence like suitability (munasaba) and refinement of the ratio legis (tanqih al-manat) and others. For us, the apparent (zahir) is the independence of the cause in causality, and whenever the independent cause exists, the effect exists, so generality is necessary in the loci of the cause. And this generality is not by wording, otherwise his statement 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness' would necessitate the freeing of all black slaves from his slaves because it would then be like 'I freed every black person'. And the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise. If you say: this consequence follows on the assumption of generality by analogy also, because it would be like 'I freed every black person' in the generality of the ruling due to the apparent independence of the cause, I say: No, because analogy has not been made a legal cause for emancipation or divorce; rather, there must be a wording that indicates it by conventional or customary signification, so it does not follow on the assumption of no generality by wording, as is not hidden from one who serves the fiqh principles. I say: there is consideration in this, by denying the implication because blackness is a qualifying cause (illa musahhiha) for emancipation, not necessitating it necessarily, unlike intoxication which is an independent cause; so generality in the independent cause does not necessitate generality in the qualifying cause. Consider: it is not complete, because if generality were by wording, the comparison of the lam al-‘illa and whatever is like it would indicate generality, and independence and implication have no role; so emancipation would be necessitated necessarily because it issued from someone eligible for emancipation with a wording indicating it conventionally or customarily. He said in the gloss: the discussion is about jural causes (al-‘ilal al-ja‘liyya), not real ones; therefore both are equal. So consider: there is further discussion. It is argued that the divine addresses, though conditioned by jural causes, contain transitive rulings; the Lawgiver placed the cause as a cause for the realization of the ruling wherever that cause exists, so the cause is implied of the ruling unlike this cause because it is not a cause for the ruling to imply it, rather for the validity of emancipation; so only its suitability for emancipation follows, not its actual occurrence. This is the truth we have established, and nothing refutes it, so ponder. The Qadi said: it is possible that the particularity of the locus is part of it, so the ruling does not go beyond it. We reply: first, this possibility is weak, so it is not considered; second, it would invalidate analogy absolutely. Those who generalize by wording said: 'Wine is forbidden due to its intoxication' is like 'the intoxicant is forbidden'; the second is general by wording, so the first is likewise. We reply: 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication' is like 'the intoxicant is forbidden' in the root of the generality of the ruling, for the ruling in both is general, not in its being (i.e., the generality) by wording. So what does not benefit you is conceded, and what benefits you is denied. How can it be conceded? It is equal to the claimant in ignorance. I say: in 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication' it is necessary to consider the universal major premise for implication, i.e., for its implication of the conditioned ruling, for a single premise does not benefit anything. So the estimation of the speech is: because it is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden. Its generality (i.e., the generality of the major premise) is by wording, because the estimated is like the uttered, in being a word qualified by generality; therefore it is by wording. Consider: it is not complete because it is possible that the purpose is to state the cause itself of the ruling, not to infer the ruling, so that the universal major premise is needed. This is the gist of the gloss. The author of Matali‘ al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: The one who says generality by wording does not mean that the form of the cause indicates generality by mutabaqa, for that is absolutely false and no sane person would intend it. Perhaps his intent is that the conditioning necessitates the existence of the ruling generally by way of textual indication (dalalat al-nass); therefore those who rejected analogy said it, and no help is sought from analogy. This is solid speech, but one must consider: if it is meant that the structure of this composition (the association with the particle of conditioning) is placed linguistically or customarily to generalize the ruling, this too is false; otherwise, his statement 'I freed him because of his blackness' would necessitate freeing all black slaves due to the issuance of a verbal composition indicating emancipation from someone eligible, as mentioned. If it is meant that stating an independent cause fit for implication necessitates generality necessarily without needing the legislation of analogy, this is exactly what the author transmits from the Hanafis and Imam Ahmad, and he chooses it: that the specification of the cause necessitates the ruling in the branch without depending on the legislation of analogy because of its clarity. So consider it, for the dispute is verbal.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) in usul al-fiqh over the nature of generality (umum) that arises when the Lawgiver strings a ruling with an explicit cause (illa). The core question: when the Shar‘i says 'Wine is haram because it is intoxicating,' does that statement itself (by its wording) generalize the ruling to all intoxicants, or does it only generalize via qiyas (analogy)? Three positions are documented: (1) Imam al-Shafi‘i and others hold it generalizes by qiyas—the wording itself does not imply generality, but the underlying cause is identified, and qiyas then extends the ruling. (2) Al-Nazzam holds the wording itself gives generality. (3) Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani holds generality is achieved neither by wording nor by qiyas automatically; rather, additional reasoning (e.g., suitability of the cause, tanqih al-manat) is needed. The Hanafi position (adopted by the author) is the first: the illa implies generality by qiyas. The proof (lana) is that the cause is independent in its causality—whenever the cause exists, the effect (ruling) exists—thus, by necessity, wherever the cause is found, the ruling applies. But this is not by wording; if it were, the example 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness' would imply freeing all black slaves, which is false by consensus. The rejoinder that even on the qiyas view, the same problem arises because the cause appears independent, is rejected because analogy in shari‘a is not itself a cause for transactions like emancipation; a verbal or customary indicator is still needed. A lengthy discussion follows on the distinction between jural causes (al-‘ilal al-ja‘liyya) and real causes, and whether the cause is independent or merely qualifying (musahhiha). The author also examines the argument that generality by wording would require a universal major premise (e.g., 'every intoxicant is haram') which is estimated as part of the statement, but this is rebutted because the purpose might be merely to state the cause, not to infer. Ultimately, the passage concludes that the dispute is verbal (lafzi) and the Hanafi view (that specification of the cause necessitates the ruling in the branch without needing formal qiyas, but still not by wording) is preferred.

Key Terms
علة (‘illa): The cause or ratio legis for a legal ruling
عموم (‘umum): Generality; the quality of a ruling applying to all individuals of a category
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction from an existing ruling to a new case due to shared cause
صيغة (sigha): The verbal form or wording of a statement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on how the ta'lil 'Wine is haram because it is intoxicating' generates generality?
A) The wording 'because it is intoxicating' is general and covers all intoxicants.
B) The ruling generalizes by qiyas: intoxication is an independent cause, so wherever it exists, the ruling applies.
C) The generality requires a separate revealed text for each intoxicant.
D) The ruling only applies to wine because the wording specifies it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the Lawgiver states a cause, the ruling extends to all cases sharing that cause by qiyas, not by the wording of the statement.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced

Refining the Argument: Independent vs Qualifying Causes and the Verbal Dispute
Refinement and resolution of the dispute through distinctions and recognition of the verbal nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an independent cause (‘illa mustaqilla) and a qualifying cause (‘illa musahhiha) and explain why the dispute over generality by wording or qiyas is ultimately verbal.

Core Idea
The distinction between independent and qualifying causes resolves the blackness example, and the dispute is verbal because all sides agree on the practical outcome.

The debate deepens as the text examines the nature of the ‘illa: is it independent or merely qualifying? The author engages with counter-arguments, including the role of a universal major premise and the idea that ta'lil might simply state the cause without inferring generality. Ultimately, the disagreement is shown to be largely verbal, with the Hanafi position affirmed as the practical standard.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counter-argument regarding the blackness example
A commentator (says 'aqūlu fīhi ta’ammul' – I see consideration in this) objects that the blackness example only refutes the wording view if the cause (blackness) is independent. But blackness is a qualifying cause (musahhiha) for emancipation, not necessitating it. The Hanafi proof used it as if it were independent, which is invalid.
This critique distinguishes between causes that necessitate the ruling (independent) and those that merely make the act suitable (qualifying).
2
Examine the rebuttal that the distinction does not matter if generality were by wording
The author responds: if generality were by wording, then even a qualifying cause would still be expressed in a sentence that appears to condition, and a wording indicating generality (like 'because of') would still imply generality regardless of independence. So the distinction does not save the wording view.
This rebuttal shows that the wording view would still absurdly require freeing all black slaves because the phrase 'because of his blackness' is a statement of cause. The problem is the verbal structure, not the type of cause.
3
Analyze the gloss on jural vs real causes
The gloss (hāshiya) argues that the discussion is about jural causes (al-‘ilal al-ja‘liyya), not real causes. In shari‘a, causes are placed by the Lawgiver and are all jural; there is no real independent vs qualifying distinction. Therefore, both intoxication and blackness, if considered as jural causes, are equal in that they are legal causes.
This shifts the ground: if both are jural, then the Hanafi argument that blackness is not a cause holds because the Lawgiver did not make it a cause for emancipation. But the opponent might argue that if the Lawgiver did make it a cause (hypothetically), then wording would generalize.
4
Consider the universal major premise argument from the wording proponents
Proponents of the wording view argue that the ta'lil 'Wine is haram because it intoxicates' implicitly includes a major premise: 'Every intoxicant is haram.' Since the estimated premise is like uttered, it is by wording. The Hanafi response: the purpose may be simply to state the cause, not to infer the ruling; thus, a major premise is not always intended.
This shows that the wording view relies on assuming an implicit general statement, which may not be part of the Lawgiver’s intent.
5
Conclude with the author's quote from Matali‘ al-Asrar
The author quotes a passage stating that the dispute is verbal (lafẓī). The one who says by wording does not mean the form mutabaqah (directly), but rather that the conditioning necessarily implies generality without needing qiyas. The Hanafi agrees that specification of the cause leads to the ruling in the branch, but without relying on the wording as a general statement.
The practical difference disappears: both sides accept that the ruling extends to all intoxicants. The dispute is over the theoretical mechanism—wording vs. qiyas—which is verbal.

صيغة دالة عليه دلالة وضعية أو عرفية، فلا يلزم على تقدير عدم العموم بالصيغة كما لا يخفى على الخادم للقواعد الفقهية (أقول فيه تأمل) بمنع الملازمة لأن السواد علة مصححة) للإعتاق غير مستلزمة) له (بالضرورة بخلاف الإسكار) فإنه علة مستقلة فلا يلزم من العموم في العلة المستلزمة العموم في العلة المصححة (فتأمل) فإنه لا يتم، لأن العموم لو كان بالصيغة لكان مقارنة اللام العليّ، وما في معناه يدل على العموم ولا دخل للاستقلال والاستلزام فيلزم العتق ضرورة أنه صدر من أهل الإعتاق صيغة دالة عليه وضعاً أو عرفاً، قال في الحاشية الكلام في العلل الجعلية لا الواقعية فحينئذ هما سواء، فتأمل ففيه كلام بعد، ووجه بأن الخطابات الإلهية وإن كانت معللة بالعلل الجعلية، لكنها متضمنة لأحكام متعدية فإن الشارع إنما وضع العلة علة لتحقق الحكم أينما وجدت تلك العلة، فالعلة ملزومة للحكم بخلاف هذه العلة لأنها ليست علة للحكم حتى تستلزمه، بل لصحة الإعتاق، فلا يلزم منه إلا صلوحه للإعتاق لا تحققه هذا والحق ما قررناه ولا يرد عليه شيء فتدبر . قال القاضي: يحتمل أن يكون خصوصية المحل جزءاً منها فلا يتجاوز الحكم غيرها (قلنا) أولاً : هذا الاحتمال (ضعيف) فلا يعتد به، وثانياً : أنه يلزم بطلان القياس مطلقاً، المعممون بالصيغة (قالوا: حرمت الخمر لإسكاره كحرمت المسكر) والثاني عام بالصيغة فكذا الأول (قلنا) حرمت الخمر للإسكار مثل حرمت المسكر ( في أصل عموم الحكم فإن الحكم فيهما عام (لا) في كونه) أي العموم (بالصيغة) فما لا يفيدكم مسلم وما يفيدكم ممنوع، وكيف يسلم فإنه مساو للمدعي في الجهالة أقول لا بد في حرمت الخمر للإسكار (من اعتبار الكبرى الكلية للاستلزام) أي لأجل استلزامه الحكم المعلل به فإن المقدمة الواحدة لا تفيد شيئاً، فتقدير الكلام هكذا لأنه مسكر ، وكل مسكر حرام وإنما) (عمومها أي عموم الكبرى (بالصيغة، لأن المقدر كالملفوظ) في كونه لفظاً متصفاً بالعموم ، فهو إذن بالصيغة (فتأمل) فإنه لا يتم، إذ يجوز أن يكون الغرض بيان نفس علة الحكم لا الاستدلال على الحكم حتى يحتاج إلى الكبرى الكلية، هذا محصل «الحاشية»، قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: القائل بالعموم بالصيغة لا يقول إن صيغة العلة دالة على العموم مطابقة، فهو باطل قطعاً ولا يليق بحال عاقل أن يريده، ولعل مقصوده، أن التعليل يقتضي ثبوت الحكم عموماً بطريق دلالة النص، ولذا قال به من نفى القياس، فلا يستعان بالقياس هذا وهذا كلام متين لكنه يجب أن ينظر في أنه إن أريد أن هيئة هذا التركيب من الاقتران بحرف التعليل موضوعة لغة أو عرفاً لتعميم الحكم، فهو أيضاً باطل، وإلا لزم في أعتقته لسواده عتق جميع السودان بصدور تركيب لفظي دال على الإعتاق ممن هو أهل له كما مر ، وإن أريد أن تبيين علة مستقلة صالحة للاستلزام يوجب العموم ضرورة بحيث لا يحتاج إلى شرع القياس فهذا بعينه ما ينقله المصنف عن الحنفية والإمام أحمد، ويختاره أن التنصيص بالعلة يوجب الحكم في الفرع من دون توقف على شرع القياس لجلاء الأمر فيه فانظره فإن النزاع لفظي .
Issue: When the Lawgiver conditions a ruling with a cause by saying: 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating', it gives generality to its loci (i.e., wherever that cause exists) by analogy (qiyas), not by the wording. The author of Matali‘ al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: Imam al-Shafi‘i (may Allah be pleased with him) stated this. And it was said: (it gives generality) by the wording, and al-Nazzam held this. And Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: originally neither by analogy nor by wording, but needs an additional evidence like suitability (munasaba) and refinement of the ratio legis (tanqih al-manat) and others. For us, the apparent (zahir) is the independence of the cause in causality, and whenever the independent cause exists, the effect exists, so generality is necessary in the loci of the cause. And this generality is not by wording, otherwise his statement 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness' would necessitate the freeing of all black slaves from his slaves because it would then be like 'I freed every black person'. And the consequent is false by consensus, so the antecedent is likewise. If you say: this consequence follows on the assumption of generality by analogy also, because it would be like 'I freed every black person' in the generality of the ruling due to the apparent independence of the cause, I say: No, because analogy has not been made a legal cause for emancipation or divorce; rather, there must be a wording that indicates it by conventional or customary signification, so it does not follow on the assumption of no generality by wording, as is not hidden from one who serves the fiqh principles. I say: there is consideration in this, by denying the implication because blackness is a qualifying cause (illa musahhiha) for emancipation, not necessitating it necessarily, unlike intoxication which is an independent cause; so generality in the independent cause does not necessitate generality in the qualifying cause. Consider: it is not complete, because if generality were by wording, the comparison of the lam al-‘illa and whatever is like it would indicate generality, and independence and implication have no role; so emancipation would be necessitated necessarily because it issued from someone eligible for emancipation with a wording indicating it conventionally or customarily. He said in the gloss: the discussion is about jural causes (al-‘ilal al-ja‘liyya), not real ones; therefore both are equal. So consider: there is further discussion. It is argued that the divine addresses, though conditioned by jural causes, contain transitive rulings; the Lawgiver placed the cause as a cause for the realization of the ruling wherever that cause exists, so the cause is implied of the ruling unlike this cause because it is not a cause for the ruling to imply it, rather for the validity of emancipation; so only its suitability for emancipation follows, not its actual occurrence. This is the truth we have established, and nothing refutes it, so ponder. The Qadi said: it is possible that the particularity of the locus is part of it, so the ruling does not go beyond it. We reply: first, this possibility is weak, so it is not considered; second, it would invalidate analogy absolutely. Those who generalize by wording said: 'Wine is forbidden due to its intoxication' is like 'the intoxicant is forbidden'; the second is general by wording, so the first is likewise. We reply: 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication' is like 'the intoxicant is forbidden' in the root of the generality of the ruling, for the ruling in both is general, not in its being (i.e., the generality) by wording. So what does not benefit you is conceded, and what benefits you is denied. How can it be conceded? It is equal to the claimant in ignorance. I say: in 'Wine is forbidden due to intoxication' it is necessary to consider the universal major premise for implication, i.e., for its implication of the conditioned ruling, for a single premise does not benefit anything. So the estimation of the speech is: because it is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden. Its generality (i.e., the generality of the major premise) is by wording, because the estimated is like the uttered, in being a word qualified by generality; therefore it is by wording. Consider: it is not complete because it is possible that the purpose is to state the cause itself of the ruling, not to infer the ruling, so that the universal major premise is needed. This is the gist of the gloss. The author of Matali‘ al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: The one who says generality by wording does not mean that the form of the cause indicates generality by mutabaqa, for that is absolutely false and no sane person would intend it. Perhaps his intent is that the conditioning necessitates the existence of the ruling generally by way of textual indication (dalalat al-nass); therefore those who rejected analogy said it, and no help is sought from analogy. This is solid speech, but one must consider: if it is meant that the structure of this composition (the association with the particle of conditioning) is placed linguistically or customarily to generalize the ruling, this too is false; otherwise, his statement 'I freed him because of his blackness' would necessitate freeing all black slaves due to the issuance of a verbal composition indicating emancipation from someone eligible, as mentioned. If it is meant that stating an independent cause fit for implication necessitates generality necessarily without needing the legislation of analogy, this is exactly what the author transmits from the Hanafis and Imam Ahmad, and he chooses it: that the specification of the cause necessitates the ruling in the branch without depending on the legislation of analogy because of its clarity. So consider it, for the dispute is verbal.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) in usul al-fiqh over the nature of generality (umum) that arises when the Lawgiver strings a ruling with an explicit cause (illa). The core question: when the Shar‘i says 'Wine is haram because it is intoxicating,' does that statement itself (by its wording) generalize the ruling to all intoxicants, or does it only generalize via qiyas (analogy)? Three positions are documented: (1) Imam al-Shafi‘i and others hold it generalizes by qiyas—the wording itself does not imply generality, but the underlying cause is identified, and qiyas then extends the ruling. (2) Al-Nazzam holds the wording itself gives generality. (3) Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani holds generality is achieved neither by wording nor by qiyas automatically; rather, additional reasoning (e.g., suitability of the cause, tanqih al-manat) is needed. The Hanafi position (adopted by the author) is the first: the illa implies generality by qiyas. The proof (lana) is that the cause is independent in its causality—whenever the cause exists, the effect (ruling) exists—thus, by necessity, wherever the cause is found, the ruling applies. But this is not by wording; if it were, the example 'I freed Zayd because of his blackness' would imply freeing all black slaves, which is false by consensus. The rejoinder that even on the qiyas view, the same problem arises because the cause appears independent, is rejected because analogy in shari‘a is not itself a cause for transactions like emancipation; a verbal or customary indicator is still needed. A lengthy discussion follows on the distinction between jural causes (al-‘ilal al-ja‘liyya) and real causes, and whether the cause is independent or merely qualifying (musahhiha). The author also examines the argument that generality by wording would require a universal major premise (e.g., 'every intoxicant is haram') which is estimated as part of the statement, but this is rebutted because the purpose might be merely to state the cause, not to infer. Ultimately, the passage concludes that the dispute is verbal (lafzi) and the Hanafi view (that specification of the cause necessitates the ruling in the branch without needing formal qiyas, but still not by wording) is preferred.

Key Terms
علة مستقلة (‘illa mustaqilla): An independent cause that necessarily entails the ruling whenever it exists.
علة مصححة (‘illa musahhiha): A qualifying cause that makes the act suitable for the ruling but does not necessitate it.
علة جعلية (‘illa ja‘liyya): A jural cause placed by the Lawgiver, as opposed to a real (natural) cause.
نزاع لفظي (niza‘ lafẓī): A verbal dispute where the disagreement is over terminology or theoretical explanation, not over practical rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dispute over generality by wording or qiyas considered verbal?
A) Because both sides agree that the ruling extends to all intoxicants, and the difference is only in how that extension is explained.
B) Because the wording proponents have no evidence and rely on weak arguments.
C) Because the Hanafis changed their position over time.
D) Because the Quran is unclear on the matter.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over how a legislative cause generates generality is ultimately verbal; the Hanafi school holds that it is by qiyas (or direct specification), not by the wording itself, but both views yield the same practical result.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Intending Three in 'طلقي نفسك': Inclusive Indication (Tadamun)
Examining whether intention for three divorces is valid in 'طلقي نفسك' via inclusive indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of tadamun (inclusive indication) to determine whether intention for multiple divorces is valid in the phrase 'طلقي نفسك'.

Core Idea
A verb's inclusive indication of its verbal noun allows intention for multiple divorces, unlike mere logical implication.

This passage examines a subtle linguistic point: when a husband says 'طلقي نفسك' (divorce yourself), does his inner intention for three divorces count? The author argues that the verb 'طلقي' implicitly includes the verbal noun 'طلاق' through inclusive indication (tadamun), and since a verbal noun can be qualified (e.g., by number), the intention is valid. We will unpack how tadamun differs from other forms of implication and why it permits such manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic issue
Recognize that the verb 'طلقي' linguistically contains its verbal noun 'طلاق' via tadamun (inclusive indication). The word 'طلقي' means 'bring about a divorce', so the verbal noun is part of its meaning.
Tadamun is distinct from talazum (necessary consequence) – tadamun is a direct inclusion, not a separate implication.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule on tadamun
According to Hanafi usul, what is indicated by tadamun (inclusive indication) is directly intended by the speaker. Unlike logical implication, it is 'noted' by the speaker and thus can be manipulated (e.g., intended for a specific number).
The author mentions that the matter (material) indicates the verbal noun by correspondence (mutabaqa), and this indication is noted by the speaker.
3
Address the counterexample 'لا آكل'
The objection is raised: in 'لا آكل' (I do not eat), the verb also includes the verbal noun 'أكل', yet one cannot intend specific types of eating. The author answers that the verbal noun in 'لا آكل' is the absolute act of eating (movement of jaws), not open to qualification, while the verbal noun in 'طلقي' is 'طلاق' which has specific types (bain, raj'i) and can be qualified.
This distinction rests on the nature of the verbal noun: 'طلاق' is a legal term with divisible categories, whereas 'أكل' is a natural act not divided in custom.
4
Confirm the ruling
Thus, in 'طلقي نفسك', the husband's intention for three divorces is valid because the inclusive indication of the verbal noun allows manipulation by number. The ruling is that three divorces occur if so intended.
This conclusion is specific to the phrase 'طلقي نفسك' and does not apply to other expressions like 'أنت طالق' (handled in next lesson).

Source Text

فرع آخر في قول الرجل لامرأته : (طلقي نفسك، يصح فيه نية الثلاث فوهم أنه من باب إضمار الكل) فإن الطلاق مضمر ليس ملفوظاً فأجيب بأنه متضمن للمصدر (لغة فلا إضمار (لأن معناه أوجدي طلاقاً) فيكون المصدر مدلولاً لغة بالتضمن (والمصدر يصح فيه نية الثلاث، كما في : أنت الطلاق، أو طالق طلاقاً فإنه يصح فيه نية الثلاث، ولقائل أن يقول : إنه قد سبق أن اللفظ المفرد لا يدل على معان كثيرة بواحد، وإن الدلالة التضمنية متحدة المطابقة فلا التصرف فيها، بل مثل هذه الدلالة مثل الالتزامية المنطقية، ولك أن تجيب عنه بأنه لا شك أن هذه الدلالة ملحوظة للمتكلم فإن ها هنا دلالتين المادة والهيئة والمادة تدل مطابقة على المصدر، وحينئذ فصح التصرف فيه بخلاف المدلول الالتزامي المنطقي الغير الملحوظ للمتكلم أن الحكم باتحاد الدلالتين أمر عسير جداً فتذكر (أقول) هذا (منقوض بنحو لا آكل فإنه أيضاً متضمن للمصدر، فيصح فيه نية أكل دون أكل ومأكول دون مأكول (فتأمل) إشارة إلى الجواب بأن المصدر المتضمن فيه نفس الأكل المصدر للفعل، وهو مطلق من حيث لا يجوز تقييده بحال، بخلاف طلقي، فإن المتضمن فيه مصدر آخر وهو الطلاق صالح لأن هو يتصرف فيه فيراد به طلاق دون طلاق هذا محصول ما في «الحاشية». ولك أن تفرق بأن أفراد الأكل باعتبار تقييده بمأكول دون مأكول كأكل التفاحة أو أكل الخبز لا يصح أن التقييد بالمفعول لم يعتبر ولم يلاحظ ، وأما أفراده باعتبار ذاته، وهي أنواع حركة اللحيين، فلا يلتفت إليه فيه عرفاً، بخلاف الطلاق من البائن والرجعي فتدبر (ونقض في المشهور بطالق) فإنه إذا قيل : أنت طالق لا يصح نية الثلاث، مع أن المصدر متضمن فيه أيضاً ودفع بأن الطلاق) المذكور فيه (وصفها وهو أثر التطليق، وتكرر الأثر بتكرر المؤثر) الذي هو التطليق (والمؤثر غیر (مكرر) فلا يتكرر الطلاق الذي من صفات المرأة، وإنما لا يتكرر التطليق المؤثر (لأن الثابت لتصحيح الخبرية من باب المقتضى فلا يقبل العموم وتفصيله أن : أنت طالق، وطلقتك، إخبار عن اتصاف المرأة بالطلاق فلا بد من وقوعه قبيل هذا الخبر ليصدق، فيثبت إيقاع من الزوج لتصحيح الخبرية، فهو من باب المقتضى الغير المقدر ولا عموم له ولا تعدد مع أنه قد مر ينوي لأن فيه فلا يتعدد الطلاق هذا، وفيه نظر فإنا سلمنا الخبرية، وسلمنا أن الإيقاع من باب المقتضى لتصحيح الخبرية، لكن لا يلزم منه أن لا تصح نية الثلاث فإنه لما نوى الثلاث قصد الحكاية اتصاف المرأة بالطلقات الثلاث، فلا بد من اعتبار إيقاعها كذلك لتصحيح الخبرية ولا ينافي قولهم المقتضى لا يعم ولا يتعدد ما قلنا لأن المراد أنه لا يعم عموماً يقبل التخصيص ولا يتعدد تعدداً يقبل النقصان، ثم إن ما ذكرتم بعينه جار في : أنت طالق طلاقاً فإن التطليق ههنا أيضاً من باب المقتضى، فينبغي أن لا ولا يتعدد فتأمل (وقد يقال أنت طالق (منقول إلى إنشاء (الواحدة عرفاً فما فوقها من الاثنين والثلاث (لا لفظ له) فلا يصح نية الزائد، وعلى هذا لا يرد شيء لكن إن دل دليل على هذا النقل وإنما هو دعوى محض فتدبر
Another branch: regarding a man's statement to his wife: 'Divorce yourself,' it is valid to intend three divorces, and it was misunderstood that it is from the category of implicit inclusion. For divorce is implicit, not uttered. It was answered that it includes the verbal noun linguistically, so there is no implication. Because its meaning is 'bring about a divorce,' so the verbal noun is indicated linguistically by inclusion, and the verbal noun can validly have the intention of three, as in 'you are the divorce' or 'you are divorced with a divorce,' for it is valid to intend three. And one may object that it was previously stated that a single word does not indicate many meanings by one word, and that the inclusive indication is unified in correspondence so there is no manipulation in it; rather, such indication is like the logical implicative. And you may answer by saying that there is no doubt that this indication is noted by the speaker, for there are here two indications: matter and form. The matter indicates the verbal noun by correspondence, and then manipulation in it is valid, unlike the logical implicative meaning not noted by the speaker. The judgment of the unity of the two indications is very difficult, so remember. He said: This is refuted by something like 'I do not eat,' for it also includes the verbal noun, so it would be valid to intend eating without eating and food without food. So reflect. This is a reference to the answer that the verbal noun included in it is the very eating as the verbal noun of the verb, and it is absolute in that it cannot be qualified by any condition, unlike 'divorce yourself,' for the verbal noun included in it is another verbal noun, i.e., divorce, which is suitable for manipulation so that one can intend one divorce but not another. This is the gist of what is in the 'Hashiyah.' And you can differentiate by saying that the individuals of eating in terms of its restriction by a certain food, like eating an apple or eating bread, it is not valid because restriction by the object is not considered and not noted. But its individuals in terms of its essence, which are types of movement of the jaws, are not attended to in custom, unlike divorce which is of the irrevocable and revocable types, so reflect. And it is objected in the well-known case with 'you are divorced,' for if one says 'you are divorced,' it is not valid to intend three, even though the verbal noun is also included in it. And it is repelled by saying that the divorce mentioned in it is a description of her, and it is the effect of the repudiation. The repetition of the effect occurs by the repetition of the cause, which is the repudiation, and the cause is not repeated, so the divorce which is an attribute of the woman is not repeated. Rather, the effecting repudiation is not repeated because what is established to correct the declarative statement is from the category of what is required (al-muqtada), so it does not accept generality. The detail is that 'you are divorced' and 'I have divorced you' are reports of the woman being characterized by divorce, so it must have occurred prior to this report for it to be true. Thus, the occurrence of the divorce from the husband is established to correct the declarative statement. So it is from the category of what is required and not estimated, and it has no generality and no multiplicity, even though it has been mentioned before that one intends because in it, therefore, divorce is not multiplied. But there is a consideration: We concede the declarative nature and we concede that the occurrence is from the category of what is required to correct the declarative statement. However, it does not necessarily follow that the intention of three is not valid. For when he intends three, he intends to report the woman being characterized by three divorces, so it is necessary to consider the occurrence likewise to correct the declarative statement. And it does not contradict their statement that the required does not include generally and does not multiply. For what is meant is that it does not include a generality that accepts specification and does not multiply a multiplicity that accepts reduction. Then, what you mentioned applies exactly to 'you are divorced with a divorce,' for the repudiation here also is from the category of what is required, so it should not include nor multiply, so reflect. And it may be said that 'you are divorced' is transferred to a performative statement customarily for one divorce, so what is above it, i.e., two and three, has no wording, so the intention of more is not valid. On this, nothing is refuted, but if a proof indicates this transfer, it is merely a claim, so reflect.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion within Hanafi usul about the validity of intending three divorces when using indirect expressions. The focus is on the phrase 'طلقي نفسك' (divorce yourself). The author explains that because the verb 'طلقي' implicitly contains the verbal noun (طلاق), one can validly intend three divorces, just as in explicit phrases like 'أنت طالق طلاقًا'. An objection is raised using the example of 'لا آكل' (I do not eat) where the implicit verbal noun is absolute and cannot be qualified, but the answer differentiates because eating is conceptually unrestricted while divorce has specific types. The discussion then critiques the common position that 'أنت طالق' (you are divorced) does not allow intention of three, arguing that it is a declarative statement requiring a prior occurrence, and the required element (mqtada) does not accept generality. However, the author shows that if one intends three, the declarative statement would require three occurrences, but the principle is that the required does not multiply. The passage concludes with the opinion that 'أنت طالق' is customarily performative for one divorce only. This reflects the Hanafi method of analyzing linguistic implications and intentions.

Key Terms
تضمن (tadamun): Inclusive indication; a word's meaning includes its constituent meaning, e.g., the verb 'طلقي' includes the verbal noun 'طلاق'.
مقتضى (muqtada): That which is required for the truth of a statement; e.g., for 'أنت طالق' to be true, the divorce must have already occurred, so the occurrence is a muqtada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is intention for three divorces valid in 'طلقي نفسك'?
A) Because the word 'طلقي' is a command.
B) Because the verb includes the verbal noun 'طلاق' by inclusive indication (tadamun).
C) Because the husband explicitly stated the intention.
D) Because the phrase is customarily performative for three divorces.

Key Takeaway
In 'طلقي نفسك', inclusive indication (tadamun) of the verbal noun permits a husband's intention for three divorces to be effective.
