Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Core Hanafi Position: Can a Prohibited Act Be Legally Valid?
Dispute over whether acts prohibited inherently can be legally valid according to Hanafis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that an act prohibited for its essence (manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi) is not legally valid (sharʿī), and identify the two meanings of sharʿī that resolve the inter-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
An act prohibited for its essence cannot be a legally valid composite because it lacks good elements and positive legal effects.

This passage opens a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: does the term sharʿī (legally valid) apply to acts that are inherently prohibited? The Hanafi school answers no, but the three other schools object. To clarify, the Hanafis distinguish between two meanings of sharʿī—what the Law permits and a composite reality of good elements producing effects. Understanding this distinction is key to the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
Locate the opening sentence: 'al-manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi la yakunu sharʿiyyan'. This is the thesis.
2
Define sharʿī according to Hanafis
Find the definition: a reality composed of good pillars and conditions that the Lawgiver considers and upon which He arranges specific rulings (thamarāt).
Look for phrases like 'al-ḥaqīqah allatī iʿtabarahā al-shāriʿ'.
3
State the opposing view
The three imams (Malik, Shafiʿi, Ahmad) argue that acts like zinā and ghasb are defined only by the Law, so they are legally valid yet prohibited.
4
Apply the Hanafi response: two meanings
Sharʿī can mean (1) what is permitted or (2) a composite reality. Opponents use meaning (1); Hanafis insist on meaning (2).
Note the phrase 'yutlaqu ʿalā maʿnayayn'.
5
Conclude: why zinā is not sharʿī in the Hanafi sense
Zinā lacks the composition of good elements and does not produce positive legal effects; only punishment is attached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهي عنه لعينه لا يكون شرعياً عندنا والشرعي الذي تعلق به النهي ليس منهياً لعينه، بل لوصف أو مجاور (خلافاً للأئمة الثلاثة) وفسر الشرعي بما لا يدرك إلا بالشرع والحسي خلافه، ويرد عليه أن الزنا لا يدرك إلا بالشرع فإنه إيلاج في فرج محرم خال عن الشبهة، والفرج المحرم لا يدرك إلا بإبانة الشرع وكذا الغصب أخذ مال الغير تغلباً، والتغلب لا يدرك إلا بالشرع مع أنهما حسيان منهيان لأعيانهما، والحق ما فسره بعض المحققين من الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع بما هو شارع ورتب عليها أحكاماً مخصوصة، كالصلاة والصوم والنكاح والبيع وغير ذلك، وأما الزنا فلم يعتبرها موجبة لثمرات بل رتب عليه الحد، وكذا الغصب كما ورد في الأخبار الصحيحة وللعاهر الحجر ولا حق لعرق ظالم لنا أن كل مشروع حسن ولا شيء من المنهي عنه لعينه بحسن فلا شيء من المشروع بمنهي عنه (أما الثانية فبالاتفاق وبالضرورة (وأما الأولى) وهي أن كل مشروع حسن (فلأن التشريع إنما هو لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الذي هو مناط السعادة الأبدية فلا يكون مثل هذا الشيء (قبيحاً) لعينه بل مرضياً) في ذاته، وإن جاز أن يقارنه القبيح فيقبح لأجله، وههنا بحث قد استصعبه بعض الأعلام، وهو أن الشرعي يطلق على معنيين أحدهما : ما أجازه الشارع، وظاهر أن هذا لا يكون قبيحاً لذاته، والثاني ما مر فإن أريد بالمشروع المعنى الأول فمسلم، لكن غاية ما لزم أن ما أجازه الشارع لا يكون منهياً لعينه وليس هو مطلوبكم، وإن أردتم الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع، فالصغرى ممنوعة وليس التشريع بهذا المعنى لصلاح المعاش والمعاد بل يجوز أن يعتبرها الشارع حقيقة كالصوم مثلاً يكون بعض أنواعه كما في سوى العيدين، والتشريق حسنة وبعضها كصيام هذه الأيام قبيحة لأعيانها فينهى عن هذا البعض لعينه وكذلك الصلاة في الشرع الأركان المخصوصة بعضها حسنة كما إذا استجمعت الشروط ووقعت في غير الأوقات المكروهة، وبعضها قبيحة لذاتها كفائت الشروط، أو الواقعة في الوقت المكروه كما مر، فقد ظهر أن هذا الدليل مغلطة باشتراك الاسم لكن الأمر غير خفي على البصير الحاذق أن اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور حسنة لا تكون قبيحة وأما اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور قبيحة لا يكون سبباً لثمرة، ثم النهي عنها لقبحها لا يليق بحكمته كيف ويكفي فيه النهي عن أجزائه التي هي أمور حسية، واعتبار حقيقة مؤتلفة من هذه الأجزاء لأجل النهي لغو لا يليق بالحكمة بل اعتبار حقيقة، كذلك لا يكون إلا ليترتب عليها ثمرات في نظر المخترع لهذه الحقيقة، وهو المعنى بالحسن ههنا ولا يتصف به المنهي عنه لذاته ويكون هذا الاعتبار الموجب لترتب الثمرات إنما يكون لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الموجب للسعادة قطعاً، وهذا وإن لم يقنع به المجادل لكن يقنع المناظر المسترشد، ثم سلك المصنف مسلكاً آخر منقولاً عن الإمام الهمام محمد رحمة الله عليه وارتضى به الإمام فخر الإسلام وأشار إليه صاحب الهداية يرد عليه ما ذكر ومحصله أن الحقيقة المعتبرة شرعاً إذا خلت عن الثمرات ممتنعة، ولا تصلح لتعلق النهي وتفصيله ما أفاد بقوله : (أقول : التحقيق أن الأفعال الشرعية أمور إما وجودات، أو بعضها وجود وبعضها عدم) وليس الكل عدمات (وهي وإن كانت حسنة عقلاً لكن ما كانت موجبة لأحكامها التي هي ثمراتها إلا بعد جعل الشارع) من حيث هو شارع ( واعتباره وهو) أي هذا الجعل والاعتبار (نحو) من الإيجاد في نفس الأمر، فهو جعل بعضها ركناً وبعضها شرطاً، فجاءت حقائق كلية مركبة من تلك الأفعال (متحصلة) في نفس الأمر (موجبة لأحكامها المقصودة منها بعد وجود الشرائط المشروطة بها ووضع لها أسماء مخصوصة أو استعمل فيها مجازاً وعلمها أي علم تلك الحقائق (للناس بتوسط الرسل الذين هم لسان الحق صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين) خصوصاً على سيدنا محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين، وإذا علمت أن الحقيقة الشرعية ليست إلا ما اعتبرها الشارع مؤتلفة من أركان مشروطة بشروط فليس فسادها وقبحها الذاتي إلا بفقدان شرط أو ركن والحقيقة الفاقدة الركن أو الشرط من المستحيلات بالذات فلا تصلح لتعلق النهي كما مر قبل، فما يتراءى فيه تعلق النهي به فلأجل عروض وصف أو مجاور لا لذاته إلا فيما إذا علم من خارج أن الركن أو هذا الشرط مفقود فحينئذ يتصرف في النهي أو المنهي كما مر وفي «الحاشية» وقد ظهر التحقيق أن الحقيقة الشرعية مجعولة حادثة ولها حقيقة متحصلة عند الشارع وهي بالأسماء الشرعية لا الصورة فقط، وأن جعل بعض الأمور ركناً وبعضها شرطاً توقيفي لا يدرك بالعقل وأن المستجمعة منها للأركان والشرائط لا تنعدم بعروض عارض، لأن العلة التامة لوجودها موجودة في نفس الأمر، فمن قال : أن لا صوم في يوم صوم في يوم العيد فعليه جعل كونه في غير يوم العيد من ركنه أو شرطه، وهو خلاف الإجماع، فلا نهي عنه إلا باعتبار وصف عارض، فلا يكون منهياً عنه لذاته، ومنشأ ذلك أن كل أمر اعتبر ركناً أو شرطاً حسن، فهو من حيث نفسه ليس منشأ للفساد، بل لمجاور انتهت قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية: لا ينفع المخالفين، فإن طورهم أن حقيقة الصلاة والصوم مثلاً تلك الأركان وهي ليست في حد ذاتها حسنة ولا قبيحة، بل هي مع بعض الأحوال قبيحة ومع بعضها حسنة، أو يقول : إن الحقيقة الصومية هي التقييدات ككونها في غير العيد، ودعوى أنه خلاف الإجماع غير مسموع لعدم البينة عليه هذا هو الذي عليه الإمام حجة الإسلام هذا والتحقيق على ما عند هذا العبد أن ههنا مطلبين: الأول: أن النهي لا يتعلق بالحقيقة الشرعية بالذات، فلا تكون هي منهية عنها بالذات، ولا شك أن الحقيقة الشرعية هي الأفعال الحسنة التي اعتبرها الشارع مجتمعة مشروطة بشروط خاصة، وما ذكر المصنف واف به وهذه الحقيقة لا تصلح للقبح الذاتي والنهي عنها بالذات، لأن الشيء المستجمع للأركان والشرائط موجبة لثمراتها البتة، فهي مشروعة، فلا تكون غير مشروعة بالذات للقبح إلا فاقدة أحد هذه الأمور، فهي من المستحيلات فلا يتعلق بها النهي لذاتها
That which is prohibited for its essence is not, according to us, legally valid (shar'i); and the legally valid to which prohibition attaches is not prohibited for its essence, but rather due to a description or adjacency (contrary to the three imams). And [some] interpreted 'legally valid' as that which is not known except through the Law, as opposed to the sensory. It is objected against this that adultery (zina) is not known except through the Law, for it is insertion into a prohibited vulva devoid of ambiguity, and the prohibited vulva is not known except by declaration of the Law; similarly, usurpation (ghasb) is taking another's property by force, and force is not known except through the Law, while both are sensory and prohibited for their essences. The correct [definition] is what some investigators have interpreted: the reality which the Lawgiver considered as a Lawgiver and upon which he arranged specific rulings, like prayer, fasting, marriage, sale, and so forth. As for adultery, He did not consider it as productive of fruits but rather assigned to it the fixed punishment; similarly usurpation, as reported in authentic traditions: 'and for the fornicator is the stone, and there is no right for the unjust root.' For us [the proof] is that every legally valid act (mashru') is good (hasan) and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. As for the second [premise, i.e., nothing prohibited for its essence is good], it is by consensus and necessity. As for the first, that every legally valid act is good—because legalization is for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife, which is the basis of eternal happiness—so such a thing cannot be ugly (qabih) in its essence but is rather pleasing in itself, although it is possible that ugliness may accompany it and it becomes ugly due to that. Here there is a research that some eminent scholars found difficult: that 'legally valid' is used in two senses: one: what the Lawgiver permits; it is obvious that this cannot be ugly for its essence. The second: what has passed. If by 'legally valid' the first meaning is intended, then it is granted, but the necessary consequence is that what the Lawgiver permits is not prohibited for its essence, and this is not what you seek. If you intend the reality that the Lawgiver considered, then the minor premise is denied; legalization in this sense is not for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife; rather, it is possible that the Lawgiver considers a reality like fasting, some types of which are good (as on days other than the two Eids and the days of Tashriq) and some types are ugly for their essences, such as fasting on those days, so this part is prohibited for its essence. Similarly, prayer in the Law consists of specific pillars; some are good when conditions are met and not in disliked times, and some are ugly for their essences, like those lacking conditions or occurring in a disliked time, as mentioned. So it has become clear that this proof is a fallacy due to homonymy, but the matter is not hidden from the sharp-eyed intelligent person: that the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of good things cannot be ugly; but the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of ugly things cannot be a cause for fruit. Then prohibition due to its ugliness is not befitting His wisdom, for it would suffice to prohibit its parts which are sensory matters. And considering a reality composed of these parts for the sake of prohibition is futile and not befitting wisdom. Rather, considering such a reality only exists for the sake of arranging fruits in the view of the inventor of this reality, and that is what is meant by 'good' here, and the prohibited for its essence does not possess that. This consideration that necessitates the arrangement of fruits is surely for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife which necessitates happiness. Even if this does not convince the disputant, it convinces the seeker of guidance. Then the author took another approach transmitted from Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, which Imam Fakhr al-Islam approved and the author of al-Hidayah indicated, to refute what was mentioned. Its gist: that a reality considered by the Law, if devoid of fruits, is impossible and is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition. Its detail is what he expressed: 'I say: the verification is that legal acts are matters, either existences or some existences and some non-existences'—and not all are non-existences—'and they, although good in reason, were not productive of their rulings which are their fruits except after the Lawgiver's making and consideration, and that making and consideration is a kind of bringing into existence in reality, so He made some as pillars and some as conditions; thus came universal realities composed of those acts, realized in reality, productive of their intended rulings after the existence of the conditions attached to them, and He assigned them specific names or used them metaphorically, and made those realities known to people through the messengers who are the tongue of truth, especially our master Muhammad and his family and companions.' When you know that the legal reality is nothing but what the Lawgiver considered as composed of pillars conditioned with conditions, then its corruption and essential ugliness are only due to the loss of a condition or pillar, and the reality lacking a pillar or condition is inherently impossible, so it is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition as before. Thus, what appears to have prohibition attached to it is due to an accidental description or adjacency, not for its essence, except when it is known from outside that the pillar or condition is missing; then one deals with the prohibition or the prohibited as before. In al-Hashiyah: 'It has become clear that the legal reality is made (maj'ula) and originated, and it has an actualized reality according to the Lawgiver, and it is by the legal names, not just the form. And that making some matters pillars and some conditions is tawqifi, not known by reason. And that those realities that combine the pillars and conditions are not nullified by the occurrence of an accident, because the complete cause for their existence exists in reality. So whoever says that there is no fasting on the day of 'Eid must make being on other than the day of 'Eid a pillar or condition, which is contrary to consensus; thus prohibition is only due to an accidental description, so it is not prohibited for its essence. The origin of this is that every matter considered a pillar or condition is good; it is not in itself a source of corruption, but rather due to adjacency.' End. The one who attained the divine secrets said: 'It does not benefit the opponents, for their manner is that the reality of prayer and fasting, for example, are those pillars, and they are not good or ugly in themselves, but rather ugly in some states and good in others'; or he says: 'The fasting reality is the restrictions like being on other than 'Eid, and the claim that it is contrary to consensus is not heard because of lack of evidence.' This is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam held. And the verification according to this servant is that there are two demands: First: that prohibition does not attach to the legal reality itself, so it is not prohibited itself; and there is no doubt that the legal reality is the good acts that the Lawgiver considered combined and conditioned with specific conditions, and what the author mentioned is sufficient. This reality is not suitable for essential ugliness and prohibition on itself, because the thing that combines the pillars and conditions is certainly productive of its fruits; it is legalized (mashru'), so it cannot be non-legalized for ugliness unless it lacks one of these matters, then it is among impossibilities; thus prohibition does not attach to it for itself.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a classical usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the nature of legal acts (sharʿī) and whether something prohibited inherently (manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi) can be considered legally valid according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: an act prohibited for its own essence cannot be legally valid (sharʿī). The legal validity here means an act that the Lawgiver has recognized as a composite reality with specific rulings. Opponents (the three imams: Malik, Shafiʿi, Ahmad) argue that even acts like zinā (adultery) and ghasb (usurpation) are defined by the Law and thus are legal, yet they are prohibited. The Hanafi response refines the definition of sharʿī: it is a reality that the Lawgiver considers as consisting of good elements (pillars and conditions) and from which legal effects (thamarāt) follow, such as validity, rewards, etc. Acts like zinā and ghasb have no positive legal effects—only punishments—so they are not legally valid in this sense. The key proof is a qiyas: every legally valid act (mashruʿ) is good (hasan), and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. The second premise is established by consensus and necessity (it is self-evident that evil acts are not good). The first premise is defended by noting that legislation is for the benefit of this life and the next, which is the foundation of eternal happiness; thus a legally valid act cannot be inherently ugly. The passage then addresses a subtlety: the term 'sharʿī' can mean either 'what the Lawgiver permits' or 'the legal reality composed of specific elements.' The opponents' objection that zinā is 'legal' because only the Law defines its components is refuted by showing that zinā lacks the composite, fruitful nature of true legal acts. The second part of the passage presents an alternative argument attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (ratified by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and referenced in al-Hidayah). This argument focuses on the impossibility of a legal reality being subject to prohibition: a legal reality is a composite of existent acts (some positive, some negative) that the Lawgiver has made into meaningful constructs with pillars and conditions. Such a reality, when complete, necessarily produces its intended effects. If it lacks a pillar or condition, it is inherently impossible and thus cannot exist to be prohibited. Therefore, any prohibition that appears to relate to such an act must be due to an accidental description or adjacency, not the essence. The author concludes with his own analysis, affirming that the legal reality combines good elements and cannot carry essential prohibition; the only prohibition applicable is due to external factors.

Key Terms
المنهي عنه لعينه (al-manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi): An act prohibited due to its own inherent quality, not due to an external description or adjacency.
الشرعي (al-sharʿī): The legal reality that the Lawgiver considers as a composite of pillars and conditions producing specific rulings.
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-ḥaqīqah al-sharʿiyyah): A constructed reality (like prayer, fasting) made of prescribed elements, created by the Sharīʿah and yielding intended outcomes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is zinā (adultery) not considered a sharʿī (legally valid) act?
A) Because it is prohibited by the Quran, so it cannot be legal.
B) Because it lacks the composition of good pillars and conditions that produce beneficial legal effects.
C) Because it is a purely sensory act with no relation to the Sharīʿah.
D) Because the Sharīʿah did not define its components.

Key Takeaway
An act prohibited for its essence cannot be sharʿī because the Lawgiver's legal realities are composites of good elements producing beneficial effects.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
The Qiyas Argument: Every Mashruʿ is Good
Dispute over whether acts prohibited inherently can be legally valid according to Hanafis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Hanafi qiyas that proves an act prohibited for its essence cannot be legally valid, and analyze the objection based on the ambiguity (ishtirak) of the term 'sharʿī'.

Core Idea
The qiyas uses two premises: consensus that no prohibited act is good, and teleology that legislation aims for benefit, so every mashruʿ is good.

After stating the core position, the author presents a formal qiyas (analogical argument) to support it. This qiyas is a powerful syllogism: every legally valid act (mashruʿ) is good, and nothing prohibited for its essence is good, therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. However, a subtle objection arises based on the term 'sharʿī' being used in two senses. This lesson unpacks the qiyas, its justification, and the homonymy objection.

Guided Path
1
Write the qiyas in standard form
Major premise: 'Every mashruʿ is hasan.' Minor premise: 'Nothing manhiyy li ʿaynihi is hasan.' Conclusion: 'No mashruʿ is manhiyy li ʿaynihi.'
The Arabic: كل مشروع حسن ولا شيء من المنهي عنه لعينه بحسن فلا شيء من المشروع بمنهي عنه
2
Establish the major premise
Why is every legally valid act good? Because legislation is for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife (iṣlāḥ al-maʿāsh wa al-maʿād), which is the basis of eternal happiness. So such an act cannot be inherently ugly.
The text says: 'فلأن التشريع إنما هو لصلاح المعاش والمعاد'
3
Establish the minor premise
Nothing prohibited for its essence is good—this is by consensus and necessity (bil-ittifāq wa bil-ḍarūrah).
4
Identify the homonymy objection
The term 'sharʿī' has two meanings: (1) what the Lawgiver permits, (2) the composite reality. The major premise is only true for meaning (1). If meaning (2) is intended, the major premise is denied because a composite reality could be prohibited (e.g., fasting on ʿEid).
This is the 'mugālatah bi-ishtirāk al-ism' (fallacy of homonymy).
5
Resolve the objection using the refined definition
The author argues that the composite reality (meaning 2) is always composed of good elements and produces fruits, so it cannot be inherently ugly. Prohibition that appears is due to accidental description (waṣf ʿāriḍ) or adjacency (mujāwir).
The key phrase: 'اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور حسنة لا تكون قبيحة'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهي عنه لعينه لا يكون شرعياً عندنا والشرعي الذي تعلق به النهي ليس منهياً لعينه، بل لوصف أو مجاور (خلافاً للأئمة الثلاثة) وفسر الشرعي بما لا يدرك إلا بالشرع والحسي خلافه، ويرد عليه أن الزنا لا يدرك إلا بالشرع فإنه إيلاج في فرج محرم خال عن الشبهة، والفرج المحرم لا يدرك إلا بإبانة الشرع وكذا الغصب أخذ مال الغير تغلباً، والتغلب لا يدرك إلا بالشرع مع أنهما حسيان منهيان لأعيانهما، والحق ما فسره بعض المحققين من الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع بما هو شارع ورتب عليها أحكاماً مخصوصة، كالصلاة والصوم والنكاح والبيع وغير ذلك، وأما الزنا فلم يعتبرها موجبة لثمرات بل رتب عليه الحد، وكذا الغصب كما ورد في الأخبار الصحيحة وللعاهر الحجر ولا حق لعرق ظالم لنا أن كل مشروع حسن ولا شيء من المنهي عنه لعينه بحسن فلا شيء من المشروع بمنهي عنه (أما الثانية فبالاتفاق وبالضرورة (وأما الأولى) وهي أن كل مشروع حسن (فلأن التشريع إنما هو لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الذي هو مناط السعادة الأبدية فلا يكون مثل هذا الشيء (قبيحاً) لعينه بل مرضياً) في ذاته، وإن جاز أن يقارنه القبيح فيقبح لأجله، وههنا بحث قد استصعبه بعض الأعلام، وهو أن الشرعي يطلق على معنيين أحدهما : ما أجازه الشارع، وظاهر أن هذا لا يكون قبيحاً لذاته، والثاني ما مر فإن أريد بالمشروع المعنى الأول فمسلم، لكن غاية ما لزم أن ما أجازه الشارع لا يكون منهياً لعينه وليس هو مطلوبكم، وإن أردتم الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع، فالصغرى ممنوعة وليس التشريع بهذا المعنى لصلاح المعاش والمعاد بل يجوز أن يعتبرها الشارع حقيقة كالصوم مثلاً يكون بعض أنواعه كما في سوى العيدين، والتشريق حسنة وبعضها كصيام هذه الأيام قبيحة لأعيانها فينهى عن هذا البعض لعينه وكذلك الصلاة في الشرع الأركان المخصوصة بعضها حسنة كما إذا استجمعت الشروط ووقعت في غير الأوقات المكروهة، وبعضها قبيحة لذاتها كفائت الشروط، أو الواقعة في الوقت المكروه كما مر، فقد ظهر أن هذا الدليل مغلطة باشتراك الاسم لكن الأمر غير خفي على البصير الحاذق أن اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور حسنة لا تكون قبيحة وأما اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور قبيحة لا يكون سبباً لثمرة، ثم النهي عنها لقبحها لا يليق بحكمته كيف ويكفي فيه النهي عن أجزائه التي هي أمور حسية، واعتبار حقيقة مؤتلفة من هذه الأجزاء لأجل النهي لغو لا يليق بالحكمة بل اعتبار حقيقة، كذلك لا يكون إلا ليترتب عليها ثمرات في نظر المخترع لهذه الحقيقة، وهو المعنى بالحسن ههنا ولا يتصف به المنهي عنه لذاته ويكون هذا الاعتبار الموجب لترتب الثمرات إنما يكون لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الموجب للسعادة قطعاً، وهذا وإن لم يقنع به المجادل لكن يقنع المناظر المسترشد، ثم سلك المصنف مسلكاً آخر منقولاً عن الإمام الهمام محمد رحمة الله عليه وارتضى به الإمام فخر الإسلام وأشار إليه صاحب الهداية يرد عليه ما ذكر ومحصله أن الحقيقة المعتبرة شرعاً إذا خلت عن الثمرات ممتنعة، ولا تصلح لتعلق النهي وتفصيله ما أفاد بقوله : (أقول : التحقيق أن الأفعال الشرعية أمور إما وجودات، أو بعضها وجود وبعضها عدم) وليس الكل عدمات (وهي وإن كانت حسنة عقلاً لكن ما كانت موجبة لأحكامها التي هي ثمراتها إلا بعد جعل الشارع) من حيث هو شارع ( واعتباره وهو) أي هذا الجعل والاعتبار (نحو) من الإيجاد في نفس الأمر، فهو جعل بعضها ركناً وبعضها شرطاً، فجاءت حقائق كلية مركبة من تلك الأفعال (متحصلة) في نفس الأمر (موجبة لأحكامها المقصودة منها بعد وجود الشرائط المشروطة بها ووضع لها أسماء مخصوصة أو استعمل فيها مجازاً وعلمها أي علم تلك الحقائق (للناس بتوسط الرسل الذين هم لسان الحق صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين) خصوصاً على سيدنا محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين، وإذا علمت أن الحقيقة الشرعية ليست إلا ما اعتبرها الشارع مؤتلفة من أركان مشروطة بشروط فليس فسادها وقبحها الذاتي إلا بفقدان شرط أو ركن والحقيقة الفاقدة الركن أو الشرط من المستحيلات بالذات فلا تصلح لتعلق النهي كما مر قبل، فما يتراءى فيه تعلق النهي به فلأجل عروض وصف أو مجاور لا لذاته إلا فيما إذا علم من خارج أن الركن أو هذا الشرط مفقود فحينئذ يتصرف في النهي أو المنهي كما مر وفي «الحاشية» وقد ظهر التحقيق أن الحقيقة الشرعية مجعولة حادثة ولها حقيقة متحصلة عند الشارع وهي بالأسماء الشرعية لا الصورة فقط، وأن جعل بعض الأمور ركناً وبعضها شرطاً توقيفي لا يدرك بالعقل وأن المستجمعة منها للأركان والشرائط لا تنعدم بعروض عارض، لأن العلة التامة لوجودها موجودة في نفس الأمر، فمن قال : أن لا صوم في يوم صوم في يوم العيد فعليه جعل كونه في غير يوم العيد من ركنه أو شرطه، وهو خلاف الإجماع، فلا نهي عنه إلا باعتبار وصف عارض، فلا يكون منهياً عنه لذاته، ومنشأ ذلك أن كل أمر اعتبر ركناً أو شرطاً حسن، فهو من حيث نفسه ليس منشأ للفساد، بل لمجاور انتهت قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية: لا ينفع المخالفين، فإن طورهم أن حقيقة الصلاة والصوم مثلاً تلك الأركان وهي ليست في حد ذاتها حسنة ولا قبيحة، بل هي مع بعض الأحوال قبيحة ومع بعضها حسنة، أو يقول : إن الحقيقة الصومية هي التقييدات ككونها في غير العيد، ودعوى أنه خلاف الإجماع غير مسموع لعدم البينة عليه هذا هو الذي عليه الإمام حجة الإسلام هذا والتحقيق على ما عند هذا العبد أن ههنا مطلبين: الأول: أن النهي لا يتعلق بالحقيقة الشرعية بالذات، فلا تكون هي منهية عنها بالذات، ولا شك أن الحقيقة الشرعية هي الأفعال الحسنة التي اعتبرها الشارع مجتمعة مشروطة بشروط خاصة، وما ذكر المصنف واف به وهذه الحقيقة لا تصلح للقبح الذاتي والنهي عنها بالذات، لأن الشيء المستجمع للأركان والشرائط موجبة لثمراتها البتة، فهي مشروعة، فلا تكون غير مشروعة بالذات للقبح إلا فاقدة أحد هذه الأمور، فهي من المستحيلات فلا يتعلق بها النهي لذاتها
That which is prohibited for its essence is not, according to us, legally valid (shar'i); and the legally valid to which prohibition attaches is not prohibited for its essence, but rather due to a description or adjacency (contrary to the three imams). And [some] interpreted 'legally valid' as that which is not known except through the Law, as opposed to the sensory. It is objected against this that adultery (zina) is not known except through the Law, for it is insertion into a prohibited vulva devoid of ambiguity, and the prohibited vulva is not known except by declaration of the Law; similarly, usurpation (ghasb) is taking another's property by force, and force is not known except through the Law, while both are sensory and prohibited for their essences. The correct [definition] is what some investigators have interpreted: the reality which the Lawgiver considered as a Lawgiver and upon which he arranged specific rulings, like prayer, fasting, marriage, sale, and so forth. As for adultery, He did not consider it as productive of fruits but rather assigned to it the fixed punishment; similarly usurpation, as reported in authentic traditions: 'and for the fornicator is the stone, and there is no right for the unjust root.' For us [the proof] is that every legally valid act (mashru') is good (hasan) and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. As for the second [premise, i.e., nothing prohibited for its essence is good], it is by consensus and necessity. As for the first, that every legally valid act is good—because legalization is for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife, which is the basis of eternal happiness—so such a thing cannot be ugly (qabih) in its essence but is rather pleasing in itself, although it is possible that ugliness may accompany it and it becomes ugly due to that. Here there is a research that some eminent scholars found difficult: that 'legally valid' is used in two senses: one: what the Lawgiver permits; it is obvious that this cannot be ugly for its essence. The second: what has passed. If by 'legally valid' the first meaning is intended, then it is granted, but the necessary consequence is that what the Lawgiver permits is not prohibited for its essence, and this is not what you seek. If you intend the reality that the Lawgiver considered, then the minor premise is denied; legalization in this sense is not for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife; rather, it is possible that the Lawgiver considers a reality like fasting, some types of which are good (as on days other than the two Eids and the days of Tashriq) and some types are ugly for their essences, such as fasting on those days, so this part is prohibited for its essence. Similarly, prayer in the Law consists of specific pillars; some are good when conditions are met and not in disliked times, and some are ugly for their essences, like those lacking conditions or occurring in a disliked time, as mentioned. So it has become clear that this proof is a fallacy due to homonymy, but the matter is not hidden from the sharp-eyed intelligent person: that the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of good things cannot be ugly; but the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of ugly things cannot be a cause for fruit. Then prohibition due to its ugliness is not befitting His wisdom, for it would suffice to prohibit its parts which are sensory matters. And considering a reality composed of these parts for the sake of prohibition is futile and not befitting wisdom. Rather, considering such a reality only exists for the sake of arranging fruits in the view of the inventor of this reality, and that is what is meant by 'good' here, and the prohibited for its essence does not possess that. This consideration that necessitates the arrangement of fruits is surely for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife which necessitates happiness. Even if this does not convince the disputant, it convinces the seeker of guidance. Then the author took another approach transmitted from Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, which Imam Fakhr al-Islam approved and the author of al-Hidayah indicated, to refute what was mentioned. Its gist: that a reality considered by the Law, if devoid of fruits, is impossible and is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition. Its detail is what he expressed: 'I say: the verification is that legal acts are matters, either existences or some existences and some non-existences'—and not all are non-existences—'and they, although good in reason, were not productive of their rulings which are their fruits except after the Lawgiver's making and consideration, and that making and consideration is a kind of bringing into existence in reality, so He made some as pillars and some as conditions; thus came universal realities composed of those acts, realized in reality, productive of their intended rulings after the existence of the conditions attached to them, and He assigned them specific names or used them metaphorically, and made those realities known to people through the messengers who are the tongue of truth, especially our master Muhammad and his family and companions.' When you know that the legal reality is nothing but what the Lawgiver considered as composed of pillars conditioned with conditions, then its corruption and essential ugliness are only due to the loss of a condition or pillar, and the reality lacking a pillar or condition is inherently impossible, so it is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition as before. Thus, what appears to have prohibition attached to it is due to an accidental description or adjacency, not for its essence, except when it is known from outside that the pillar or condition is missing; then one deals with the prohibition or the prohibited as before. In al-Hashiyah: 'It has become clear that the legal reality is made (maj'ula) and originated, and it has an actualized reality according to the Lawgiver, and it is by the legal names, not just the form. And that making some matters pillars and some conditions is tawqifi, not known by reason. And that those realities that combine the pillars and conditions are not nullified by the occurrence of an accident, because the complete cause for their existence exists in reality. So whoever says that there is no fasting on the day of 'Eid must make being on other than the day of 'Eid a pillar or condition, which is contrary to consensus; thus prohibition is only due to an accidental description, so it is not prohibited for its essence. The origin of this is that every matter considered a pillar or condition is good; it is not in itself a source of corruption, but rather due to adjacency.' End. The one who attained the divine secrets said: 'It does not benefit the opponents, for their manner is that the reality of prayer and fasting, for example, are those pillars, and they are not good or ugly in themselves, but rather ugly in some states and good in others'; or he says: 'The fasting reality is the restrictions like being on other than 'Eid, and the claim that it is contrary to consensus is not heard because of lack of evidence.' This is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam held. And the verification according to this servant is that there are two demands: First: that prohibition does not attach to the legal reality itself, so it is not prohibited itself; and there is no doubt that the legal reality is the good acts that the Lawgiver considered combined and conditioned with specific conditions, and what the author mentioned is sufficient. This reality is not suitable for essential ugliness and prohibition on itself, because the thing that combines the pillars and conditions is certainly productive of its fruits; it is legalized (mashru'), so it cannot be non-legalized for ugliness unless it lacks one of these matters, then it is among impossibilities; thus prohibition does not attach to it for itself.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a classical usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the nature of legal acts (sharʿī) and whether something prohibited inherently (manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi) can be considered legally valid according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: an act prohibited for its own essence cannot be legally valid (sharʿī). The legal validity here means an act that the Lawgiver has recognized as a composite reality with specific rulings. Opponents (the three imams: Malik, Shafiʿi, Ahmad) argue that even acts like zinā (adultery) and ghasb (usurpation) are defined by the Law and thus are legal, yet they are prohibited. The Hanafi response refines the definition of sharʿī: it is a reality that the Lawgiver considers as consisting of good elements (pillars and conditions) and from which legal effects (thamarāt) follow, such as validity, rewards, etc. Acts like zinā and ghasb have no positive legal effects—only punishments—so they are not legally valid in this sense. The key proof is a qiyas: every legally valid act (mashruʿ) is good (hasan), and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. The second premise is established by consensus and necessity (it is self-evident that evil acts are not good). The first premise is defended by noting that legislation is for the benefit of this life and the next, which is the foundation of eternal happiness; thus a legally valid act cannot be inherently ugly. The passage then addresses a subtlety: the term 'sharʿī' can mean either 'what the Lawgiver permits' or 'the legal reality composed of specific elements.' The opponents' objection that zinā is 'legal' because only the Law defines its components is refuted by showing that zinā lacks the composite, fruitful nature of true legal acts. The second part of the passage presents an alternative argument attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (ratified by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and referenced in al-Hidayah). This argument focuses on the impossibility of a legal reality being subject to prohibition: a legal reality is a composite of existent acts (some positive, some negative) that the Lawgiver has made into meaningful constructs with pillars and conditions. Such a reality, when complete, necessarily produces its intended effects. If it lacks a pillar or condition, it is inherently impossible and thus cannot exist to be prohibited. Therefore, any prohibition that appears to relate to such an act must be due to an accidental description or adjacency, not the essence. The author concludes with his own analysis, affirming that the legal reality combines good elements and cannot carry essential prohibition; the only prohibition applicable is due to external factors.

Key Terms
المنهي عنه لعينه (al-manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi): An act prohibited due to its own inherent quality, not due to an external description or adjacency.
الشرعي (al-sharʿī): The legal reality that the Lawgiver considers as a composite of pillars and conditions producing specific rulings.
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-ḥaqīqah al-sharʿiyyah): A constructed reality (like prayer, fasting) made of prescribed elements, created by the Sharīʿah and yielding intended outcomes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the minor premise of the Hanafi qiyas: 'Nothing prohibited for its essence is good'?
A) It is derived from a Quranic verse.
B) It is established by consensus and necessity (bil-ittifāq wa bil-ḍarūrah).
C) It is proven by the Sunnah.
D) It is a rational axiom accepted by all schools.

Key Takeaway
The qiyas is valid once we distinguish essential from accidental prohibition, and define 'sharʿī' as a composite reality that cannot be inherently ugly.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
Imam Muhammad's Argument: The Impossibility of Prohibiting a Complete Legal Reality
Dispute over whether acts prohibited inherently can be legally valid according to Hanafis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a complete legal reality (having all pillars and conditions) cannot be prohibited for itself according to Imam Muhammad, and apply this to the case of fasting on ʿEid.

Core Idea
A complete legal reality necessarily produces its fruits; lacking a pillar or condition makes it impossible, so only accidental prohibitions exist.

The author presents a second, more rigorous argument transmitted from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, approved by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and referenced in al-Hidayah. This argument focuses on the nature of legal realities as constructs (majʿūlah) made by the Lawgiver, composed of pillars and conditions. Such a reality, when complete, is necessarily productive of its intended effects; if it lacks a pillar or condition, it is inherently impossible (mumtaniʿ) and cannot exist to be prohibited. Therefore, any prohibition that appears to attach to a legal act must be due to an accidental description or adjacency, not the essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of legal realities
Legal realities (like prayer, fasting) are not mere sensory acts; they are composites made by the Lawgiver (majʿūlah), consisting of certain existences and non-existences arranged as pillars and conditions.
The text says: 'التحقيق أن الأفعال الشرعية أمور إما وجودات، أو بعضها وجود وبعضها عدم'
2
Identify the role of the Lawgiver's making (jaʿl)
The Lawgiver's act of making and considering these composites brings them into legal existence. He designates some parts as pillars (arkān) and some as conditions (shurūṭ). This is tawqīfī (revelation-based), not rational.
3
Realize that a complete composite necessarily produces its fruits
When all pillars and conditions are present, the legal reality is 'muḥaṣṣalah' (actualized) and 'mūjibah li-aḥkāmihā' (productive of its rulings). It cannot be otherwise; the complete cause for its existence and effects is present.
The text: 'المستجمعة للأركان والشرائط لا تنعدم بعروض عارض'
4
Conclude that a complete reality cannot be prohibited for itself
Since the complete reality is necessarily good and productive, it cannot be the object of essential prohibition. Any prohibition that appears is due to an accidental description (waṣf ʿāriḍ) or adjacency (mujāwir).
5
Apply to the case of fasting on ʿEid
Fasting on ʿEid has all the pillars and conditions of fasting except the condition of being on a non-ʿEid day. But that condition is not part of the legal reality; it is an external restriction. The prohibition is therefore due to adjacency, not essence.
The text: 'فمن قال : أن لا صوم في يوم صوم في يوم العيد فعليه جعل كونه في غير يوم العيد من ركنه أو شرطه، وهو خلاف الإجماع'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهي عنه لعينه لا يكون شرعياً عندنا والشرعي الذي تعلق به النهي ليس منهياً لعينه، بل لوصف أو مجاور (خلافاً للأئمة الثلاثة) وفسر الشرعي بما لا يدرك إلا بالشرع والحسي خلافه، ويرد عليه أن الزنا لا يدرك إلا بالشرع فإنه إيلاج في فرج محرم خال عن الشبهة، والفرج المحرم لا يدرك إلا بإبانة الشرع وكذا الغصب أخذ مال الغير تغلباً، والتغلب لا يدرك إلا بالشرع مع أنهما حسيان منهيان لأعيانهما، والحق ما فسره بعض المحققين من الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع بما هو شارع ورتب عليها أحكاماً مخصوصة، كالصلاة والصوم والنكاح والبيع وغير ذلك، وأما الزنا فلم يعتبرها موجبة لثمرات بل رتب عليه الحد، وكذا الغصب كما ورد في الأخبار الصحيحة وللعاهر الحجر ولا حق لعرق ظالم لنا أن كل مشروع حسن ولا شيء من المنهي عنه لعينه بحسن فلا شيء من المشروع بمنهي عنه (أما الثانية فبالاتفاق وبالضرورة (وأما الأولى) وهي أن كل مشروع حسن (فلأن التشريع إنما هو لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الذي هو مناط السعادة الأبدية فلا يكون مثل هذا الشيء (قبيحاً) لعينه بل مرضياً) في ذاته، وإن جاز أن يقارنه القبيح فيقبح لأجله، وههنا بحث قد استصعبه بعض الأعلام، وهو أن الشرعي يطلق على معنيين أحدهما : ما أجازه الشارع، وظاهر أن هذا لا يكون قبيحاً لذاته، والثاني ما مر فإن أريد بالمشروع المعنى الأول فمسلم، لكن غاية ما لزم أن ما أجازه الشارع لا يكون منهياً لعينه وليس هو مطلوبكم، وإن أردتم الحقيقة التي اعتبرها الشارع، فالصغرى ممنوعة وليس التشريع بهذا المعنى لصلاح المعاش والمعاد بل يجوز أن يعتبرها الشارع حقيقة كالصوم مثلاً يكون بعض أنواعه كما في سوى العيدين، والتشريق حسنة وبعضها كصيام هذه الأيام قبيحة لأعيانها فينهى عن هذا البعض لعينه وكذلك الصلاة في الشرع الأركان المخصوصة بعضها حسنة كما إذا استجمعت الشروط ووقعت في غير الأوقات المكروهة، وبعضها قبيحة لذاتها كفائت الشروط، أو الواقعة في الوقت المكروه كما مر، فقد ظهر أن هذا الدليل مغلطة باشتراك الاسم لكن الأمر غير خفي على البصير الحاذق أن اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور حسنة لا تكون قبيحة وأما اعتبار الشارع حقيقة مؤتلفة من أمور قبيحة لا يكون سبباً لثمرة، ثم النهي عنها لقبحها لا يليق بحكمته كيف ويكفي فيه النهي عن أجزائه التي هي أمور حسية، واعتبار حقيقة مؤتلفة من هذه الأجزاء لأجل النهي لغو لا يليق بالحكمة بل اعتبار حقيقة، كذلك لا يكون إلا ليترتب عليها ثمرات في نظر المخترع لهذه الحقيقة، وهو المعنى بالحسن ههنا ولا يتصف به المنهي عنه لذاته ويكون هذا الاعتبار الموجب لترتب الثمرات إنما يكون لصلاح المعاش والمعاد الموجب للسعادة قطعاً، وهذا وإن لم يقنع به المجادل لكن يقنع المناظر المسترشد، ثم سلك المصنف مسلكاً آخر منقولاً عن الإمام الهمام محمد رحمة الله عليه وارتضى به الإمام فخر الإسلام وأشار إليه صاحب الهداية يرد عليه ما ذكر ومحصله أن الحقيقة المعتبرة شرعاً إذا خلت عن الثمرات ممتنعة، ولا تصلح لتعلق النهي وتفصيله ما أفاد بقوله : (أقول : التحقيق أن الأفعال الشرعية أمور إما وجودات، أو بعضها وجود وبعضها عدم) وليس الكل عدمات (وهي وإن كانت حسنة عقلاً لكن ما كانت موجبة لأحكامها التي هي ثمراتها إلا بعد جعل الشارع) من حيث هو شارع ( واعتباره وهو) أي هذا الجعل والاعتبار (نحو) من الإيجاد في نفس الأمر، فهو جعل بعضها ركناً وبعضها شرطاً، فجاءت حقائق كلية مركبة من تلك الأفعال (متحصلة) في نفس الأمر (موجبة لأحكامها المقصودة منها بعد وجود الشرائط المشروطة بها ووضع لها أسماء مخصوصة أو استعمل فيها مجازاً وعلمها أي علم تلك الحقائق (للناس بتوسط الرسل الذين هم لسان الحق صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين) خصوصاً على سيدنا محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين، وإذا علمت أن الحقيقة الشرعية ليست إلا ما اعتبرها الشارع مؤتلفة من أركان مشروطة بشروط فليس فسادها وقبحها الذاتي إلا بفقدان شرط أو ركن والحقيقة الفاقدة الركن أو الشرط من المستحيلات بالذات فلا تصلح لتعلق النهي كما مر قبل، فما يتراءى فيه تعلق النهي به فلأجل عروض وصف أو مجاور لا لذاته إلا فيما إذا علم من خارج أن الركن أو هذا الشرط مفقود فحينئذ يتصرف في النهي أو المنهي كما مر وفي «الحاشية» وقد ظهر التحقيق أن الحقيقة الشرعية مجعولة حادثة ولها حقيقة متحصلة عند الشارع وهي بالأسماء الشرعية لا الصورة فقط، وأن جعل بعض الأمور ركناً وبعضها شرطاً توقيفي لا يدرك بالعقل وأن المستجمعة منها للأركان والشرائط لا تنعدم بعروض عارض، لأن العلة التامة لوجودها موجودة في نفس الأمر، فمن قال : أن لا صوم في يوم صوم في يوم العيد فعليه جعل كونه في غير يوم العيد من ركنه أو شرطه، وهو خلاف الإجماع، فلا نهي عنه إلا باعتبار وصف عارض، فلا يكون منهياً عنه لذاته، ومنشأ ذلك أن كل أمر اعتبر ركناً أو شرطاً حسن، فهو من حيث نفسه ليس منشأ للفساد، بل لمجاور انتهت قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية: لا ينفع المخالفين، فإن طورهم أن حقيقة الصلاة والصوم مثلاً تلك الأركان وهي ليست في حد ذاتها حسنة ولا قبيحة، بل هي مع بعض الأحوال قبيحة ومع بعضها حسنة، أو يقول : إن الحقيقة الصومية هي التقييدات ككونها في غير العيد، ودعوى أنه خلاف الإجماع غير مسموع لعدم البينة عليه هذا هو الذي عليه الإمام حجة الإسلام هذا والتحقيق على ما عند هذا العبد أن ههنا مطلبين: الأول: أن النهي لا يتعلق بالحقيقة الشرعية بالذات، فلا تكون هي منهية عنها بالذات، ولا شك أن الحقيقة الشرعية هي الأفعال الحسنة التي اعتبرها الشارع مجتمعة مشروطة بشروط خاصة، وما ذكر المصنف واف به وهذه الحقيقة لا تصلح للقبح الذاتي والنهي عنها بالذات، لأن الشيء المستجمع للأركان والشرائط موجبة لثمراتها البتة، فهي مشروعة، فلا تكون غير مشروعة بالذات للقبح إلا فاقدة أحد هذه الأمور، فهي من المستحيلات فلا يتعلق بها النهي لذاتها
That which is prohibited for its essence is not, according to us, legally valid (shar'i); and the legally valid to which prohibition attaches is not prohibited for its essence, but rather due to a description or adjacency (contrary to the three imams). And [some] interpreted 'legally valid' as that which is not known except through the Law, as opposed to the sensory. It is objected against this that adultery (zina) is not known except through the Law, for it is insertion into a prohibited vulva devoid of ambiguity, and the prohibited vulva is not known except by declaration of the Law; similarly, usurpation (ghasb) is taking another's property by force, and force is not known except through the Law, while both are sensory and prohibited for their essences. The correct [definition] is what some investigators have interpreted: the reality which the Lawgiver considered as a Lawgiver and upon which he arranged specific rulings, like prayer, fasting, marriage, sale, and so forth. As for adultery, He did not consider it as productive of fruits but rather assigned to it the fixed punishment; similarly usurpation, as reported in authentic traditions: 'and for the fornicator is the stone, and there is no right for the unjust root.' For us [the proof] is that every legally valid act (mashru') is good (hasan) and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. As for the second [premise, i.e., nothing prohibited for its essence is good], it is by consensus and necessity. As for the first, that every legally valid act is good—because legalization is for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife, which is the basis of eternal happiness—so such a thing cannot be ugly (qabih) in its essence but is rather pleasing in itself, although it is possible that ugliness may accompany it and it becomes ugly due to that. Here there is a research that some eminent scholars found difficult: that 'legally valid' is used in two senses: one: what the Lawgiver permits; it is obvious that this cannot be ugly for its essence. The second: what has passed. If by 'legally valid' the first meaning is intended, then it is granted, but the necessary consequence is that what the Lawgiver permits is not prohibited for its essence, and this is not what you seek. If you intend the reality that the Lawgiver considered, then the minor premise is denied; legalization in this sense is not for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife; rather, it is possible that the Lawgiver considers a reality like fasting, some types of which are good (as on days other than the two Eids and the days of Tashriq) and some types are ugly for their essences, such as fasting on those days, so this part is prohibited for its essence. Similarly, prayer in the Law consists of specific pillars; some are good when conditions are met and not in disliked times, and some are ugly for their essences, like those lacking conditions or occurring in a disliked time, as mentioned. So it has become clear that this proof is a fallacy due to homonymy, but the matter is not hidden from the sharp-eyed intelligent person: that the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of good things cannot be ugly; but the Lawgiver's consideration of a reality composed of ugly things cannot be a cause for fruit. Then prohibition due to its ugliness is not befitting His wisdom, for it would suffice to prohibit its parts which are sensory matters. And considering a reality composed of these parts for the sake of prohibition is futile and not befitting wisdom. Rather, considering such a reality only exists for the sake of arranging fruits in the view of the inventor of this reality, and that is what is meant by 'good' here, and the prohibited for its essence does not possess that. This consideration that necessitates the arrangement of fruits is surely for the betterment of livelihood and afterlife which necessitates happiness. Even if this does not convince the disputant, it convinces the seeker of guidance. Then the author took another approach transmitted from Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, which Imam Fakhr al-Islam approved and the author of al-Hidayah indicated, to refute what was mentioned. Its gist: that a reality considered by the Law, if devoid of fruits, is impossible and is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition. Its detail is what he expressed: 'I say: the verification is that legal acts are matters, either existences or some existences and some non-existences'—and not all are non-existences—'and they, although good in reason, were not productive of their rulings which are their fruits except after the Lawgiver's making and consideration, and that making and consideration is a kind of bringing into existence in reality, so He made some as pillars and some as conditions; thus came universal realities composed of those acts, realized in reality, productive of their intended rulings after the existence of the conditions attached to them, and He assigned them specific names or used them metaphorically, and made those realities known to people through the messengers who are the tongue of truth, especially our master Muhammad and his family and companions.' When you know that the legal reality is nothing but what the Lawgiver considered as composed of pillars conditioned with conditions, then its corruption and essential ugliness are only due to the loss of a condition or pillar, and the reality lacking a pillar or condition is inherently impossible, so it is not suitable for the attachment of prohibition as before. Thus, what appears to have prohibition attached to it is due to an accidental description or adjacency, not for its essence, except when it is known from outside that the pillar or condition is missing; then one deals with the prohibition or the prohibited as before. In al-Hashiyah: 'It has become clear that the legal reality is made (maj'ula) and originated, and it has an actualized reality according to the Lawgiver, and it is by the legal names, not just the form. And that making some matters pillars and some conditions is tawqifi, not known by reason. And that those realities that combine the pillars and conditions are not nullified by the occurrence of an accident, because the complete cause for their existence exists in reality. So whoever says that there is no fasting on the day of 'Eid must make being on other than the day of 'Eid a pillar or condition, which is contrary to consensus; thus prohibition is only due to an accidental description, so it is not prohibited for its essence. The origin of this is that every matter considered a pillar or condition is good; it is not in itself a source of corruption, but rather due to adjacency.' End. The one who attained the divine secrets said: 'It does not benefit the opponents, for their manner is that the reality of prayer and fasting, for example, are those pillars, and they are not good or ugly in themselves, but rather ugly in some states and good in others'; or he says: 'The fasting reality is the restrictions like being on other than 'Eid, and the claim that it is contrary to consensus is not heard because of lack of evidence.' This is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam held. And the verification according to this servant is that there are two demands: First: that prohibition does not attach to the legal reality itself, so it is not prohibited itself; and there is no doubt that the legal reality is the good acts that the Lawgiver considered combined and conditioned with specific conditions, and what the author mentioned is sufficient. This reality is not suitable for essential ugliness and prohibition on itself, because the thing that combines the pillars and conditions is certainly productive of its fruits; it is legalized (mashru'), so it cannot be non-legalized for ugliness unless it lacks one of these matters, then it is among impossibilities; thus prohibition does not attach to it for itself.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a classical usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the nature of legal acts (sharʿī) and whether something prohibited inherently (manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi) can be considered legally valid according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: an act prohibited for its own essence cannot be legally valid (sharʿī). The legal validity here means an act that the Lawgiver has recognized as a composite reality with specific rulings. Opponents (the three imams: Malik, Shafiʿi, Ahmad) argue that even acts like zinā (adultery) and ghasb (usurpation) are defined by the Law and thus are legal, yet they are prohibited. The Hanafi response refines the definition of sharʿī: it is a reality that the Lawgiver considers as consisting of good elements (pillars and conditions) and from which legal effects (thamarāt) follow, such as validity, rewards, etc. Acts like zinā and ghasb have no positive legal effects—only punishments—so they are not legally valid in this sense. The key proof is a qiyas: every legally valid act (mashruʿ) is good (hasan), and nothing prohibited for its essence is good; therefore no legally valid act is prohibited for its essence. The second premise is established by consensus and necessity (it is self-evident that evil acts are not good). The first premise is defended by noting that legislation is for the benefit of this life and the next, which is the foundation of eternal happiness; thus a legally valid act cannot be inherently ugly. The passage then addresses a subtlety: the term 'sharʿī' can mean either 'what the Lawgiver permits' or 'the legal reality composed of specific elements.' The opponents' objection that zinā is 'legal' because only the Law defines its components is refuted by showing that zinā lacks the composite, fruitful nature of true legal acts. The second part of the passage presents an alternative argument attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (ratified by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and referenced in al-Hidayah). This argument focuses on the impossibility of a legal reality being subject to prohibition: a legal reality is a composite of existent acts (some positive, some negative) that the Lawgiver has made into meaningful constructs with pillars and conditions. Such a reality, when complete, necessarily produces its intended effects. If it lacks a pillar or condition, it is inherently impossible and thus cannot exist to be prohibited. Therefore, any prohibition that appears to relate to such an act must be due to an accidental description or adjacency, not the essence. The author concludes with his own analysis, affirming that the legal reality combines good elements and cannot carry essential prohibition; the only prohibition applicable is due to external factors.

Key Terms
المنهي عنه لعينه (al-manhiyy ʿanhu li ʿaynihi): An act prohibited due to its own inherent quality, not due to an external description or adjacency.
الشرعي (al-sharʿī): The legal reality that the Lawgiver considers as a composite of pillars and conditions producing specific rulings.
الحقيقة الشرعية (al-ḥaqīqah al-sharʿiyyah): A constructed reality (like prayer, fasting) made of prescribed elements, created by the Sharīʿah and yielding intended outcomes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's argument, why is the prohibition on fasting on ʿEid not an essential prohibition?
A) Because fasting is not a legal act on ʿEid.
B) Because the prohibition is due to the adjacency of the day (mujāwir), not the essence of fasting.
C) Because the Lawgiver did not consider fasting on ʿEid as a legal reality.
D) Because consensus says any prohibition must be essential.

Key Takeaway
A complete legal reality is necessarily good and productive; it cannot be essentially prohibited—only accidental prohibitions exist.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
12 min
Speculative Dalala and the Hadd for Liwat: A Hanafi Usul Case Study
Explaining zanni dalala via kaffarah for murder and false oath, then Hanafi internal dispute on hadd for sodomy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a legal indication is speculative (zanni) and apply the Hanafi principle that such indication cannot override a clear text, using the examples of kaffarah for intentional murder and the hadd for liwat.

Core Idea
Speculative manat cannot establish a hadd or abrogate a clear text; only qat'i manat yields certainty.

In this passage, the author explores the nature of speculative indication (dalala zanniya) and its consequences for legal derivation. Through two concrete examples—the obligation of kaffarah for intentional murder and the hadd penalty for liwat—he demonstrates how different understandings of the manat (effective cause) lead to legitimate ikhtilaf even within the same madhhab. This lesson will walk you through the distinction between qat'i and zanni manat and how it determines the force of a legal argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples of speculative dalala in the text
The author first uses the Shafi'i argument for kaffarah on intentional murder and false oath, then the intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for liwat. Both hinge on whether the manat is certain (qat'i) or speculative (zanni).
The kaffarah example shows how a different understanding of the manat (deterrence vs. compensation) yields different rulings.
2
Analyze the kaffarah example: why is the Shafi'i qiyas considered zanni?
The Shafi'i posits the manat as deterrence (zajr), but the Hanafi author argues it is compensation (tilafi). Since intentional sins are pure major sins, compensation is not needed; moreover, deterrence would require a compulsive penalty, not optional expiation.
This shows that the manat is not self-evident, making the dalala speculative.
3
Analyze the liwat dispute: what is the contested manat?
The Sahibayn and three imams hold that the manat for hadd is 'pouring fluid in a forbidden desired place', which applies to liwat even more strongly. Abu Hanifa holds the manat is 'destruction of a soul' (due to child without lineage), absent in liwat.
Also, natural repulsion weakens the 'desired' aspect in liwat, further challenging the manat.
4
Apply the principle: why does speculative dalala not justify hadd?
The author cites Quran 4:16 as a clear text prescribing only tazir for liwat. A speculative dalala (qiyas on zina) cannot abrogate or override an explicit text. Hence, Abu Hanifa's position is that no hadd is imposed.
This underscores the Hanafi principle that zanni dalala cannot establish a fixed penalty (hadd).

Source Text

وقد تكون الدلالة (ظنية) إذا كان المناط مظنوناً أو وجوده في المسكوت (وذلك كإيجاب الشافعي الكفارة في القتل العمد واليمين الغموس بنص الخطأ) الموجب للكفارة فيه (و) بنص (غير الغموس) وهي المنعقدة لفهمه أن المناط الزجر والعمد والغموس أولى به من الخطأ والمنعقدة (مع احتمال أن لا يكون المناط ثمة الزجر بل (التلافي) لما صدر به التساهل التثبت حتى أدى إلى إهلاك النفس المحترمة ولما صدر من انتهاك ما أكده باسم الله تعالى، فلا يلزم في العمد والغموس لأنهما كبيرتان ،محضتان ولا يلزم من محو شيء ذنباً هو أعلى منه كيف نفس الخطأ لا ذنب فيه، وكذا في الحلف على شيء يريد فعله، وإنما يسري ذنب عدم التثبت وخلف الوعد المؤكد، وبما قررنا اندفع أن الخطأ لا ذنب فيه، فلا يحتاج إلى التلافي والزجر على هذا ثم نقول : بل الظاهر أن الكفارة موضوعة للتلافي لأنها ستارة كاسمها والمناسب للزجر ما يجري عليه من الإمام جبراً حتى ينزجر، لا ما يكون في اختياره إن شاء أتى به وإلا لا . ومن البين أن من ارتكب القتل العمد أو الغموس كيف ينزجر بوجوب شيء لو تركه عصى فلا وجه فيهما للانزجار فافهم، وقد يقال: الكفارة في الغموس عند الشافعي بالعبارة. فإن المراد بقوله تعالى: بِمَا عَقَدتُمُ الْأَيْمَنَ ﴾ [المائدة: ۸۹] العقد باليمين، وهذا عام للغموس والمنعقدة كليهما، وسيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ما يكفي لهذا المقام فانتظر (ولما جاز خفاؤها جاز الاختلاف فيها لكن لا يكون فهم المناط مختلفاً أيضاً (ففرع أبو يوسف ومحمد كالأئمة الثلاثة وجوب الحد باللواطة) غير الزوجة والأمة وأما مع معهما فلا حد فيه عندهما أيضاً على دلالة نص وجوبه بالزنا، لأن المناط سفح الماء في محل محرم مشتهى والحرمة) في محل اللواطة (قوية فوق محل الزنا، لأنه يمكن أن يحل بالنكاح دون محلها، وسفح الماء فيها فوقه في الزنا فهي مثل الزنا في إيجاب الحد (وأبو حنيفة جعل المناط لإيجاب الحد في الزنا إهلاك نفس معنى) فإنه في الزنا يكون الولد غير ثابت النسب فهو هالك وقوة الحرمة يعارضها كمال الشهوة فإن الشهوة في الزنا من الطرفين بخلافها في اللواطة، وأيضاً ينفر الطبع السليم عنها لما فيها من الاستقذار، فيكون قضاء شهوة في غير محل مشتهى من وجه فهذا يرجع إلى منع وجود المناط فيها، أو إلى كون المناط المذكور مناطاً فتأمل، وقد سمعت من مطلع الأسرار الإلهية حين اشتغالي بقراءة التلويح عليه قدس سره أن قوله تعالى: وَالذَانِ يَأْتِيَنهَا مِنكُمْ فَتَادُوهُمَا فَإن تَابَ وَأَصْلَحَا فَأَعْرِضُوا عَنْهُمَا إِنَّ الله كَانَ تَوَّابًا رَّحِيمًا ﴾ [النساء : ١٦] أريد به اللواطة ويؤيده ذكر حكم الزنا في الآية السابقة عليه ويشهد عليه صيغة اللذان ومنكم وحكي هذا أيضاً عن مجاهد، فعلى هذا يظهر بعبارة هذه الآية أن لا حد مقدر فيها بل فيها الإيذاء تعزيراً وتأديباً، وهو يختلف بحال الفاعل، ومن ادعى وجوب الحد فيه فعليه بيان انتساخه ودونه خرط القتاد، والدلالة لا تصلح ناسخة للعبارة خصوصاً مثل هذه الدلالة المظنونة الضعيفة فافهم. ثم اعلم أن ادعاء الدلالة
And the indication may be speculative (zanni) if the manat is speculative or its existence in the unspoken [case] is speculative. For example, al-Shafi'i's obligation of kaffarah for intentional murder and the false oath (al-yameen al-ghamoos) by the text on accidental [killing] which obligates kaffarah, and by the text on non-false oath (al-mun'aqida). He understood that the manat is deterrence (zajr), and intentional [acts] and the false oath are more deserving of it than accidental and non-false. But there is the possibility that the manat there is not deterrence but compensation (tilafi) for the lack of caution that led to destruction of a respected soul or the violation of what is affirmed by Allah's name. Thus it does not necessarily apply to intentional murder and false oath because they are pure major sins, and it is not necessary that expiation for a sin that is higher [in severity] than another sin be based on the same ruling, since accidental [killing] has no sin. Similarly, for an oath about something one intends to do, the sin is only of lack of caution and breaking a confirmed promise. By what we have established, it is refuted that accidental [killing] has no sin and thus needs no compensation or deterrence. Then we say: rather, the apparent is that kaffarah is established for compensation because it is a covering, as its name indicates. What is appropriate for deterrence is something the imam imposes forcibly so that one refrains, not something within one's choice to do or not. It is clear that one who commits intentional murder or a false oath is not deterred by the obligation of something that if he leaves, he sins; there is no basis for deterrence in these cases. So understand. It may be said that the kaffarah for the false oath according to al-Shafi'i is by the text, for the meaning of His saying, the Exalted, 'for what you have contracted of oaths' (5:89) is the contract by oath, and this is general for both the false and the binding [oath]. Later, Allah willing, what suffices for this discussion will come; so wait. And when its concealment is possible, disagreement about it is possible, but understanding of the manat is also not uniform. For example, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, like the three imams, derived the obligation of the hadd penalty for liwat [sodomy] other than with wife or slave; as for with them, there is no hadd in their view either, by the indication of the text that obligates it for zina, because the manat is the pouring of fluid (safh al-ma') in a forbidden place that is desired. The prohibition in the case of liwat is stronger than in zina, because [zina] can be made lawful through marriage but not the case of liwat. And the pouring of fluid in it is even more so than in zina, so it is like zina in requiring hadd. Abu Hanifa considered the manat for obligating hadd in zina to be the destruction of a soul in meaning, because in zina the child does not have established lineage, so it is destroyed. The strength of the prohibition is counterbalanced by the completeness of desire, for desire in zina is from both parties unlike in liwat. Also, sound nature abhors it due to its filth, so satisfying desire is in a place not desired in one aspect. Thus this returns to preventing the existence of the manat in it, or that the mentioned manat is [not] the actual manat. So reflect. I have heard from the one who knows divine secrets when I was studying al-Talwih under him, may Allah sanctify his secret, that His saying, the Exalted, 'And the two who commit it among you, punish them' (4:16) is intended for liwat, supported by the mention of the ruling of zina in the preceding verse, and witnessed by the dual form 'alladhan' and 'minkum'. This was also narrated from Mujahid. According to this, by the wording of this verse, there is no prescribed hadd but rather causing harm as tazir and discipline, which varies according to the doer. Whoever claims the obligation of hadd must prove its abrogation, and that is difficult. Indication (dalala) cannot abrogate the explicit text, especially such a weak speculative indication. So understand. Then know that claiming the indication...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of dalala, now focusing on speculative (zanni) implication. The author presents the Shafi'i argument that kaffarah is wajib for intentional murder and the false oath (al-yameen al-ghamoos) by qiyas on the texts about accidental killing and non-false oaths. The Shafi'i's manat is deterrence (zajr). The Hanafi critique suggests that the actual manat is compensation (tilafi), not deterrence, and compensation is not applicable to intentional sins that are purely major sins with no need for compensation. The author also notes that the Shafi'i may rely on the general wording of verse 5:89. The main legal dispute in this lesson concerns the hadd penalty for liwat (sodomy). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), like the three imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad), hold that liwat incurs the hadd of zina, because the manat is 'pouring fluid in a forbidden place that is desired', and the prohibition of liwat is even stronger than zina since it can never become halal. Abu Hanifa disagrees; he holds that the manat for zina is 'destruction of a soul' (since the child is not attributed to the father), and in liwat there is no such destruction. Additionally, the desire in liwat is weaker because of natural repulsion, so the manat is not established or the connection is different. The author also cites a view that the verse in Surat al-Nisa (4:16) refers to liwat and only prescribes tazir (discretionary punishment), not hadd, and that abrogation would be required to impose hadd. The passage illustrates how speculative dalala can lead to valid ikhtilaf within the madhhab.

Key Terms
الدلالة الظنية (al-dalala al-zanniya): A speculative indication where the manat or its presence in the unspoken case is not certain, leading to possible disagreement.
اللواطة (al-liwata): Sodomy, an act whose legal classification is disputed: whether it incurs hadd like zina or only tazir.
التلافي (al-tilafi): Compensation; the purpose of kaffarah according to the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i view, as opposed to deterrence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Hanifa hold that liwat does not incur the hadd of zina?
A) Because the manat for zina is 'pouring fluid in a forbidden place', which applies equally to liwat.
B) Because the manat for zina is 'destruction of a soul' (as it can produce a child with unestablished lineage), which is absent in liwat.
C) Because the majority of scholars disagree with him.
D) Because liwat is not explicitly mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Speculative manat leads to legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi principle is that a zanni dalala cannot override a clear text or impose a hadd.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Fahwa as Qiyas or Independent Proof: Hanafi Debate
Debate whether fahwa is a form of qiyas or separate indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on fahwa, evaluate the opposing view that fahwa is qiyas jali, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf regarding the establishment of hudud.

Core Idea
Fahwa is intuitive linguistic understanding, not rational analogy; thus it can prove hudud where qiyas cannot.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute: is fahwa (the implicit meaning derived from textual coherence) a type of qiyas or an independent mode of dalalah? The majority of Hanafis hold it is not qiyas, while Imam al-Razi and some Hanafis consider it qiyas jali. This distinction has practical consequences for hudud punishments, which cannot be proven by qiyas but may be proven by fahwa if it is independent.

Guided Path
1
Define fahwa and the dispute
Identify what fahwa means—the implicit understanding from a text, e.g., the prohibition of hitting parents derived from 'Do not say uff to them' (17:23). Then note the central question: is this a form of qiyas?
2
Identify the two positions
State the majority view (Hanafis and Shafi'is): fahwa is not qiyas. Then state the minority view (Imam al-Razi, some Hanafis): it is qiyas jali.
3
Examine the first argument: intuitive nature
The passage argues that fahwa is intuitive (badihi) for a language expert—the understanding of the effective cause (manat) is immediate. In contrast, even clear qiyas requires a reasoning step. Therefore, fahwa is not qiyas.
The objection: the opponent may still claim it is qiyas jali, as both involve extending a ruling.
4
Examine the second argument: pre-Shariah understanding
Fahwa can be understood even by someone not adhering to any Shariah. For example, a non-Muslim would grasp that 'uff' implies prohibition of harm. Qiyas only becomes a valid legal proof after Shariah establishes its authority.
The rebuttal: qiyas as reasoning can exist before Shariah; its authority in Shariah comes later. However, qiyas jali does not depend on Shariah for its validity.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf: hudud
The practical consequence: if fahwa is not qiyas, hudud can be proven by fahwa. If it is qiyas, hudud cannot be proven by it (since qiyas is not accepted for hudud). The author notes that some scholars say there is no real dispute on this point regarding hudud.
This shows that the classification affects which proofs are accepted in serious punishments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: جمهور الحنفية والشافعية على أن الفحوى ليس بقياس، وقيل هو قياس جلي، واختاره الإمام الرازي من الشافعية وبعض منا أيضاً. قيل فائدة الخلاف أن الحدود تثبت به عند من قال إنه ليس قياساً، بخلاف من قال إنه قياس. قال صاحب الكشف: قد سمعت بعض شيوخي الذي كان من الثقات أنه لم يختلف في ثبوت الحدود به، وإنما الخلاف في ثبوت الحدود بالقياس الخفي. لنا أولاً: أن الفحوى بديهي، ولهذا ثبتت به الحدود، ولا شيء من القياس كذلك أي بديهياً مثبتاً للحدود. وفيه ما فيه: لأن الكبرى ممنوعة، لأن المخالف يدعي كونه قياساً جلياً، ويعتبر بكونه مثبتاً للحدود. هذا ولك أن تمنع الصغرى كيف وربما تكون بعض الدلالات أخفى من القياس. إلا أن يتحرر الدليل: هكذا الفحوى فهم المناط فيه بديهي للعارف باللغة، وإن كان الحكم في المسكوت نظرياً لخفاء المناط فيه، والقياس ليس كذلك، والحق أن الذي يدعي فيه كونه دلالة نظرية فهم المناط ليس دلالة حقيقية بل قياسات، ولذا لم يعمل به مشايخنا فافهم. ولنا ثانياً: القطع بالإفادة أي بإفادة الفحوى الحكم قبل شرع القياس، ولذا كان يفهم عند من لا يتدين من النهي عن التأفيف النهي عن الضرب، فلا يكون قياساً شرعياً لأنه بعد الشرع. وفيه أن الاستدلال بالقياس لا يتوقف على الشرع، فيجوز كونه قياساً مفيداً قبل الشرع، ولهذا أثبته الحكماء وسموه تمثيلاً مع أنهم غير متشرعين بشريعة. نعم اعتباره أي القياس شرعاً إنما يكون بالشرع وذلك في غير الجلي، وأما الجلي فاعتباره في الشرع لا يتوقف على الشرع أيضاً. ولنا ثالثاً: الأصل في القياس لا يكون مندرجاً في الفرع بحيث يسري حكمه إليه إجماعاً، وههنا قد يكون مثل «لا تعطه ذرة» فإنه يدل على أن لا يعطيه...
Issue: The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that fahwa is not qiyas; it is said that it is qiyas jali, and Imam al-Razi from the Shafi'is and some of us also chose that. It was said the benefit of the dispute is that hudud are established by it for those who say it is not qiyas, unlike those who say it is qiyas. The author of al-Kashf said: I heard from some trustworthy teachers that there is no dispute that hudud are established by it, only a dispute regarding hudud by al-qiyas al-khafi. Our first argument: Fahwa is intuitive (badihi), and therefore hudud are established by it, and no qiyas is likewise intuitive and establishes hudud. There is discussion: because the major premise is denied, as the opponent claims it is qiyas jali and considers it as establishing hudud. You may deny the minor premise, for some indications may be more hidden than qiyas. However, if the proof is examined, fahwa's understanding of the manat is intuitive for one familiar with the language, even if the ruling on the unmentioned case is theoretical due to the hiddenness of the manat, and qiyas is not like that. The truth is that what is claimed to be theoretical indication of understanding the manat is not real indication but qiyas, and for this our mashayikh did not act upon it. Our second argument: Certainty of effect, i.e., fahwa conveys the ruling before the legislation of qiyas; thus it was understood by those who are non-religious from the prohibition of 'uff' to the prohibition of hitting, so it is not qiyas because it is pre-Shariah. But it is argued that qiyas does not depend on Shariah to be valid as a proof; it can be a valid qiyas before Shariah, and for this the philosophers used it and called it tamthil even though they are not subject to a Shariah. However, its consideration as qiyas in Shariah is only through Shariah in the case of non-jali, but jali qiyas does not depend on Shariah. Our third argument: The principle in qiyas is that the original ruling does not extend to the branch in the same wording, but here it does, like 'Do not give him a grain' which indicates not to give him anything.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of fahwa (coherence or implicit meaning) in usul al-fiqh. The central question is whether fahwa is a form of qiyas or an independent type of dalalah. The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that fahwa is not qiyas, but Imam al-Razi (Shafi'i) and some Hanafis consider it qiyas jali (clear analogy). The practical implication (thamarah) is whether hudud (fixed punishments) can be established through fahwa. According to the majority, since fahwa is not qiyas, and hudud cannot be established by qiyas, they can be established by fahwa. If it were qiyas, then hudud would not be established by it. The author then presents three arguments for the position that fahwa is not qiyas. First, fahwa is intuitive (badihi) for a language expert; the understanding of the manat is immediate, unlike qiyas which requires reasoning. Second, fahwa can be understood before the legislation of qiyas; for example, anyone who knows nothing about Shariah will understand from 'Don't say uff to them' that hitting is also prohibited. This shows fahwa is a linguistic understanding, not a qiyas that came after Shariah. Third, in qiyas, the original ruling does not include the branch by the same wording, but in fahwa, the ruling on the unmentioned case is inherent in the wording (e.g., 'do not give a grain' implies 'do not give anything'). The author acknowledges objections and concludes that what is often called theoretical indication is actually qiyas.

Key Terms
الفحوى (al-fahwa): The implicit meaning of a text that is understood by linguistic coherence, not by explicit wording.
القياس الجلي (al-qiyas al-jali): Clear analogy where the illah is obvious and the branch is closely similar to the original.
بديهي (badihi): Intuitive or self-evident; not requiring reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, why is fahwa not considered qiyas?
A) Because it can prove hudud
B) Because it is intuitive and pre-Shariah
C) Because it requires ijtihad
D) Because it is mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Fahwa is an intuitive linguistic indication that operates independently of qiyas, with concrete consequences for establishing hudud.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
